Shahadat — Testimony
intermediate
8 min
Actions That Disqualify a Witness: From Sailing to Singing
Listing specific actions that cause rejection of testimony as per Hanafi sources

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list at least five specific actions that disqualify a witness in Hanafi law and identify the exceptions that preserve adala.

Core Idea
Certain risky trades, neglect of prayer, and immoral entertainments remove a witness's integrity (adala).

In this lesson, we examine a series of rulings from Hanafi sources that identify actions leading to rejection of testimony. These range from sailing in the Indian Ocean for trade to sitting in gatherings of singing. The underlying principle is that such actions reflect a lack of moral probity (muru'a) and religious commitment, making the witness unreliable. However, exceptions exist—e.g., leaving prayer is excused if there is a valid interpretation (ta'wil). We will go through each cause and its conditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the specific actions listed
Sailing in Indian seas, trade in Persian villages, leaving congregational/Friday prayer without ta'wil, going out to see a prince, being a slave dealer, listening to singers, and being a poet who satirizes.
2
Note the source for each
The passage cites al-Qunyah, Sharh Adab al-Qadi, al-Bazzaziyya, al-Multaqat, al-Jawhara.
3
Understand the rationale
Each action signals a flaw in character: risk-taking, involvement with riba, neglect of obligation, sycophancy, dishonesty, frivolity.
4
Apply exceptions
Some actions have exceptions: sailing does not prevent testimony according to al-Qunyah; leaving prayer is excused with ta'wil; going to see the prince is allowed if for contemplation; slave dealer's testimony may be accepted if he is just.
5
Distinguish between types of poets
A poet's testimony is accepted unless he commits slander (qadhf) or satire (hija).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
منظومة ابن وهبان له وفي القنية ركوب البحر لا يمنع قبول شهادتهم. وفي شرح أدب القاضي للشهيد حسام الدين: أسباب الجرح كثيرة منها ركوب بحر الهند لأنه مخاطرة بنفسه ودينه من سكنى دار الحرب وتكثير سوادهم وعددهم لأجل المال ومثله لا يبالي بشهادة الزور، ومنها التجارة في قرى فارس لأنهم يطعمونهم الربا وهم يعلمون ولو شهد قبل أن يستشهد تسمع شهادته بعد ذلك اهـ. وفي البزازية : ولا تجوز شهادة من ترك الصلاة بجماعة إلا إذا تركها بتأويل ولا تارك الجمعة إلا بتأويل ولا تارك الصلاة ا هـ . وفي الملتقط . خلف من خرج للنظر إلى قدوم الأمير فليس بعدل وكذا من شهد على صك مقاطعة وعن النخاسين وهو ملعون وكذا كل من شهد على باطل إذا عرفوه وإلا فتقبل. وفي الجوهرة : ولا تقبل شهادة النخاس وهو الدلال إلا إذا كان عدلاً لم يكذب ولا يحلف اهـ.
ولاتقبل شهادة من يجلس مجالس الغناء أو يتبع صوت المغنية ولا من يسمع وشهادة الشاعر ما لم يقذف في شعره مقبولة إلا إذا هجا ا هـ . وقد حرر ابن وهبان مسألة الشتم والخروج لقدوم الأمير تحريراً حسناً أحببت ذكره هنا الأولى قال : والفقه في ذلك الشتم لا يخلو إما أن يكون بما فيه أو بما ليس فيه في وجهه أو في غيبته، فإن كان بما ليس فيه فهو كذب وافتراء فيفسق به سواء كان في وجهه أو في غيبته وإن كان بما فيه في غيبته فهو غيبة وأنها توجب الفسق، وإن كان في وجهه ففيه إساءة أدب أنه من صنيع رعاعة الناس وسوقتهم الذين لا مروءة لهم ولا حياء فيهم وأن ذلك مما يسقط العدالة، وكذا إذا كان السب باللعنة والإبعاد مما يفعله من لا خلاق لهم من السوقة وغيرهم ومما يؤيد ذلك ما ورد في الحديث سباب المسلم فسوق وقتاله كفر (۱). قال ابن الأثير في النهاية: السب الشتم يقال سبه يسبه سباً وسباباً . قيل هذا محمول على من سبه أو قاتل مسلماً بغير تأويل، وقيل إنما قال ذلك على جهة التغليظ لا أنه يخرجه إلى الكفر والفسق وأقول : هذا خلاف الظاهر الثانية قال قاضيخان : إذا قدم الأمير بلدة فخرج الناس وجلسوا على الطريق ينتظرون قال خلف : بطلت عدالتهم إلا أن يذهبوا للاعتبار فحينئذ لا تبطل ا هـ. وحاصله أنها لا تبطل إلا إذا كان الأمير لا يصلح للتعظيم ولم يخرجوا للاعتبار، والفقه فيه أنهم إذا خرجوا اهـ. وسلم وقبض وضمن الدرك وإن لم يكن شيء من ذلك موجوداً فيكون كذباً، ولا فرق بين الكذب بالكتاب وبين الكذب بالقول، والصحيح أنها تقبل إذا كان غالب حاله الصلاح، وما ذكر من الكذب عفو لأنهم يحققون ما كتبوا .اهـ. قوله : (من ترك الصلاة بجماعة أي إن تركها مجاناً شهراً كما سيذكره قريباً عن التهذيب . (۱) رواه البخاري في كتاب الإيمان باب .٣٦ مسلم في كتاب الإيمان حديث ١١٦. الترمذي في كتاب البر باب ٥١. ابن ماجه في كتاب الفتن باب ٤ أحمد في . مسنده (١/ ١٧٦).
The versification of Ibn Wahban belongs to him. And in al-Qunyah: Sailing does not prevent acceptance of their testimony. And in Sharh Adab al-Qadi al-Shahid Husam al-Din: The causes of disqualification [jarh] are many, among them sailing in the sea of India because it is a risk to oneself and one's religion from residing in dar al-harb and increasing their numbers for the sake of wealth, and such a person does not care about false testimony; and among them is trade in the villages of Persia because they feed them riba and they know, and if he testifies before being asked, his testimony is heard afterward, end quote. And in al-Bazzaziyya: And the testimony of one who leaves prayer in congregation is not accepted unless he leaves it with a valid interpretation [ta'wil], nor one who leaves Friday prayer except with ta'wil, nor one who leaves prayer [altogether] end quote. And in al-Multaqat: Khalaf said: Whoever goes out to look at the arrival of the prince is not just, and likewise whoever testifies to a document of qati'a (land grant?), and concerning the slave dealers, he is cursed, and likewise everyone who testifies to falsehood if they know him; otherwise it is accepted. And in al-Jawhara: And the testimony of a slave dealer [nakhkhas] (the broker) is not accepted unless he is just, does not lie, and does not swear, end quote. And the testimony of one who sits in gatherings of singing or follows the voice of a female singer, and the one who listens, is not accepted. And the testimony of a poet is accepted as long as he does not commit slander [qadhf] in his poetry, unless he satirizes [haja], end quote. And Ibn Wahban has written excellently on the issue of insults [shatm] and going out for the arrival of the prince, which I would like to mention here. He said: The fiqh of that: insult is either about something that is in the person or not in his face or in his absence; if it is about what is not in him, it is a lie and slander, so he becomes a fasiq by it, whether in his face or in his absence; if it is about what is in him in his absence, it is backbiting [ghiba] and it necessitates fisq; and if it is in his face, it is bad manners and is the doing of the rabble and commoners who have no muru'a or modesty, and that is something that removes adala. Likewise, if the curse is for execration and banishment, it is something done by those who have no noble character among the commoners and others. And supporting this is the hadith: 'Insulting a Muslim is fisq and fighting him is kufr' (1). Ibn al-Athir said in al-Nihaya: 'al-sabb' is insult; it is said sabbahu yasubbuhu sabbann wa sibaban. It was said this is interpreted as referring to one who insults or fights a Muslim without a valid interpretation, and it was said that he said it as an exaggeration, not that it takes him out of faith and fisq. And I say: this is contrary to the apparent meaning. Second: Qadikhan said: When the prince arrives at a town and people go out and sit on the road waiting, Khalaf said: Their adala is invalidated unless they go for contemplation [i'tibar]; then it is not invalidated, end quote. The summary is that it is not invalidated unless the prince is not worthy of exaltation and they did not go for contemplation. And the fiqh of it is that when they go out... end quote. And they hand over and receive and guarantee liability, and if none of that exists, then it is a lie. And there is no difference between lying by writing and lying by speech. And the correct [view] is that it is accepted if the predominant state of his is righteousness, and what is mentioned of lying is excused because they verify what they wrote, end quote. His statement: (He who leaves prayer in congregation) meaning if he leaves it without obligation for a month as he will mention soon from al-Tahdhib. (1) Narrated by al-Bukhari in Kitab al-Iman, bab. 36; Muslim in Kitab al-Iman, hadith 116; al-Tirmidhi in Kitab al-Birr, bab 51; Ibn Majah in Kitab al-Fitan, bab 4; Ahmad in his Musnad (1/176).
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqaiq continuing the list of actions that disqualify a witness. It begins by stating that sailing does not prevent acceptance of testimony according to al-Qunyah, but then Sharh Adab al-Qadi lists many causes, including sailing in the sea of India because it endangers life and religion, and trade in Persian villages because of dealing with riba. The text then addresses leaving congregational prayer: testimony of one who leaves it without a valid interpretation (ta'wil) is not accepted; this includes leaving Friday prayer and leaving prayer altogether. The passage also covers going out to see a prince (amir): if people go out to view his arrival without valid reason (like contemplation), their adala is invalidated. Additionally, the testimony of a slave dealer (nakhkhas) is not accepted unless he is just and does not lie or swear. The testimony of those who sit in singing gatherings or listen to female singers is not accepted. A poet's testimony is accepted unless he commits slander (qadhf) in his poetry or satirizes. Ibn Wahban provides a detailed analysis of insults (shatm): insulting someone with falsehood is slander and causes fisq; insulting with truth in absence is backbiting (ghiba); insulting to the face is bad manners without muru'a, which removes adala. The hadith 'سباب المسلم فسوق' supports this, and Ibn al-Athir's interpretation is discussed. Finally, Qadikhan's ruling on going out for the prince is clarified: adala is invalidated unless the prince is worthy and they go for contemplation. The passage concludes with a note that leaving prayer in congregation for a month without excuse removes adala.

Key Terms
جرح (jarh): Disqualification of a witness due to moral defects; the opposite of ta'dil.
مروءة (muru'a): Manliness or chivalry; a trait expected of a just witness; actions violating muru'a can remove adala.
تأويل (ta'wil): Valid interpretation or excuse; leaving an obligation with a valid ta'wil does not remove adala.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when does going out to see the arrival of a prince NOT invalidate one's adala?
A) When the prince is a just ruler
B) When they go for contemplation (i'tibar)
C) When they are invited by the prince
D) When they go in large numbers

Key Takeaway
A witness's adala is lost by actions that show moral negligence, such as risky trade, abandoning prayer without excuse, or engaging in frivolous entertainments.

Shahadat — Testimony
advanced
10 min
Insults (Shatm) and the Invalidation of Adala
Detailing the causes of disqualification related to insults and greeting a prince

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between slander, backbiting, and face-to-face insult, and state which type invalidates adala, as well as the exception for going to see a prince.

Core Idea
Insulting another Muslim, whether by false accusation, backbiting, or offensive language in the face, removes adala because it shows lack of muru'a.

This lesson focuses on the detailed analysis by Ibn Wahban regarding insults (shatm) and the ruling on going out to see a prince. Insults are categorized: false insult is slander (fisq), truthful insult in absence is backbiting (fisq), and insult to the face shows bad manners and lack of muru'a, also removing adala. The hadith 'Insulting a Muslim is fisq' supports this. Regarding going out to see a prince, adala is invalidated unless the prince is worthy of respect and the people go for contemplation (i'tibar). Qadikhan's ruling clarifies that the action is not always disqualifying.

Guided Path
1
Understand Ibn Wahban's classification of insults
False insult (kadhib) = slander = fisq; truthful insult in absence = ghiba = fisq; truthful insult to face = bad manners = fisq because of lacking muru'a.
2
Note the hadith evidence
'سباب المسلم فسوق' – insulting a Muslim is fisq. This supports the ruling.
3
Apply the distinction
The insult must be serious; minor insults may be excused. The hadith is interpreted literally by Hanafis.
4
Analyze the prince-watching ruling
People go out to see the prince; if the prince is not worthy and they do not go for contemplation, adala is invalidated.
5
Synthesize the principle
Both insults and excessive sycophancy show a lack of religious and moral character, thus removing adala.

Source Text

And in al-Jawhara: And the testimony of a slave dealer [nakhkhas] (the broker) is not accepted unless he is just, does not lie, and does not swear, end quote. And the testimony of one who sits in gatherings of singing or follows the voice of a female singer, and the one who listens, is not accepted. And Ibn Wahban has written excellently on the issue of insults [shatm] and going out for the arrival of the prince, which I would like to mention here. Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqaiq continuing the list of actions that disqualify a witness. It begins by stating that sailing does not prevent acceptance of testimony according to al-Qunyah, but then Sharh Adab al-Qadi lists many causes, including sailing in the sea of India because it endangers life and religion, and trade in Persian villages because of dealing with riba. The text then addresses leaving congregational prayer: testimony of one who leaves it without a valid interpretation (ta'wil) is not accepted; this includes leaving Friday prayer and leaving prayer altogether. The passage also covers going out to see a prince (amir): if people go out to view his arrival without valid reason (like contemplation), their adala is invalidated. Additionally, the testimony of a slave dealer (nakhkhas) is not accepted unless he is just and does not lie or swear. The testimony of those who sit in singing gatherings or listen to female singers is not accepted. A poet's testimony is accepted unless he commits slander (qadhf) in his poetry or satirizes. Ibn Wahban provides a detailed analysis of insults (shatm): insulting someone with falsehood is slander and causes fisq; insulting with truth in absence is backbiting (ghiba); insulting to the face is bad manners without muru'a, which removes adala. The hadith 'سباب المسلم فسوق' supports this, and Ibn al-Athir's interpretation is discussed. Finally, Qadikhan's ruling on going out for the prince is clarified: adala is invalidated unless the prince is worthy and they go for contemplation. The passage concludes with a note that leaving prayer in congregation for a month without excuse removes adala.

Key Terms
شتم (shatm): Insult; includes false insult (slander), truthful backbiting (ghiba), and face-to-face insult that shows lack of muru'a.
مروءة (muru'a): Manliness or chivalry; a trait expected of a just witness; actions violating muru'a can remove adala.
غيبة (ghiba): Backbiting; speaking about someone in their absence in a way they would dislike, even if true.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Wahban, what is the status of a person who insults another to their face with a truthful statement?
A) He is not a fasiq because it is true
B) He becomes a fasiq because it shows lack of muru'a
C) He is excused if he apologizes later
D) His adala is unaffected

Key Takeaway
Any form of insult—false, backbiting, or face-to-face—removes a witness's adala, as it contradicts the required moral probity.

Shahadat — Testimony

Causes of Jarh (Witness Disqualification)
Lists multiple grounds for rejecting testimony from various Hanafi sources.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the main causes of jarh (witness disqualification) in Hanafi law and apply them to determine if a witness's testimony is acceptable.

Core Idea
A witness is disqualified by actions that show disregard for religion or honesty.

This passage compiles rulings from several authoritative Hanafi texts identifying specific actions that undermine a witness's moral probity (ʿadālah). These actions, called causes of jarh, include risky sea travel, residence in enemy territory for profit, trade involving riba, neglect of congregational prayers, and habitual false testimony or perjury.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act
Look at the specific action mentioned in the passage (e.g., riding the Indian sea, trading in Persian villages, missing Jumu'ah).
Each action is a potential cause of jarh.
2
Check if it is listed as a cause
Determine from the passage whether the action is explicitly listed as disqualifying. Note any conflicting reports (e.g., riding the sea: Qunyah says not, Sharh Adab says yes).
If a conflict exists, the qadi must weigh the sources.
3
Look for exceptions
Some causes have exceptions: e.g., 'unless done with interpretation' for leaving congregational prayer or Jumu'ah.
An exception can restore the witness's 'adalah.
4
Apply to the witness
If the witness engaged in a cause without a valid exception, their testimony is rejected. If there is doubt or conflicting rulings, further investigation is needed.
The burden is on the witness to prove their integrity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الشهادات باب من تقبل شهادته ومن لا تقبل
منظومة ابن وهبان له وفي القنية ركوب البحر لا يمنع قبول شهادتهم. وفي شرح أدب القاضي للشهيد حسام الدين: أسباب الجرح كثيرة منها ركوب بحر الهند لأنه مخاطرة بنفسه ودينه ومن سكنى دار الحرب وتكثير سوادهم وعددهم لأجل المال ومثله لا يبالي بشهادة الزور، ومنها التجارة في قرى فارس لأنهم يطعمونهم الربا وهم يعلمون ولو شهد قبل أن يستشهد تسمع شهادته بعد ذلك اهـ. وفي البزازية: ولا تجوز شهادة من ترك الصلاة بجماعة إلا إذا تركها بتأويل ولا تارك الجمعة إلا بتأويل ولا تارك الصلاة اهـ . وفي الملتقط . خلف من خرج للنظر إلى قدوم الأمير فليس بعدل وكذا من شهد على صك مقاطعة وعن النخاسين وهو ملعون وكذا كل من شهد على باطل إذا عرفوه وإلا فتقبل. وفي الجوهرة: ولا تقبل شهادة النخاس وهو الدلال إلا إذا كان عدلاً لم يكذب ولا يحلف اهـ.
The Book of Testimonies, Chapter on Whose Testimony is Accepted and Who is Not. In the versified text of Ibn Wahban, and in al-Qunyah [it states]: riding the sea does not prevent acceptance of their testimony. And in Sharh Adab al-Qadi by al-Shahid Husam al-Din: the causes of jarh (disqualification) are many, among them riding the Indian sea because it risks one's life and religion, and dwelling in the abode of war, increasing their numbers and counts for the sake of wealth, and such a person does not care about false testimony; and among them is trading in the villages of Persia because they feed them riba while knowing, and if he testifies before being summoned, his testimony is heard after that. End quote. And in al-Bazzaziyyah: the testimony of one who leaves congregational prayer is not permissible unless he leaves it with interpretation, nor the one who leaves al-Jumu'ah except with interpretation, nor the one who leaves prayer altogether. End quote. And in al-Multaqat: Khalaf [said]: whoever goes out to look at the arrival of the ruler is not upright, and likewise whoever testifies on a document of muqata'ah, and regarding the slave dealer — he is cursed, and likewise everyone who testifies on falsehood if they know him, otherwise it is accepted. And in al-Jawharah: the testimony of the slave dealer (the broker) is not accepted unless he is upright, has not lied, and does not take oaths. End quote.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage is a collection of rulings from multiple Hanafi sources listing actions that disqualify a witness. The author begins by quoting Ibn Wahban's versified text and Qunyah: riding the sea does not automatically disqualify. Then from Sharh Adab al-Qadi, several causes of jarh are listed: riding the Indian sea (due to risk and lack of care), residing in dar al-harb for material gain, and trading in Persian villages (because they knowingly engage in riba transactions). Importantly, someone who testifies before being summoned is still heard later. From Bazzaziyyah, missing congregational prayer or Jumu'ah without valid interpretation disqualifies. From Multaqat, exiting to see the ruler's arrival and testifying on certain documents also disqualify, as do slave dealers and those known to testify falsely. From Jawharah, the broker's testimony is conditioned on his honesty. The overall message: a witness must be 'adl (just), and these actions indicate lack of 'adalah.

Key Terms
الجرح (al-jarh): Legal defect or ground for disqualifying a witness's testimony.
التأويل (al-ta'wil): A valid religious interpretation or excuse that exempts one from sin.

Key Terms from Source Passage

According to the passage, what invalidates the testimony of a person who trades in the villages of Persia?
A) They risk their life by traveling.
B) They knowingly consume riba by feeding it to others.
C) They increase the population of the enemy.
D) They are considered slave dealers.

Key Takeaway
Specific actions such as reckless sea travel, riba-related trade, neglect of prayers, and false testimony disqualify a witness by undermining moral probity.

Disqualifying Actions for Witness Testimony
Detailed expansion on disqualifying acts for witnesses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state which actions disqualify a witness in Hanafi fiqh (singing, insult, going out for a ruler) and apply the ruling to a new scenario using the conditions and proofs provided.

Core Idea
Acts that destroy muru'a or involve haram, like insult or occupying the public road, disqualify a witness.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq examines several actions that undermine a witness's 'adalah (moral integrity). The author expands on the base text by analyzing insult (sabb) and the practice of going out to greet a ruler, showing that not every instance is automatically disqualifying. Context, intent, and custom matter. We will walk through each cause and its proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Disqualifying Actions
List the four actions discussed in the passage: (1) attending singing sessions or listening to a female singer, (2) a poet's defamation (satire/hija), (3) insulting someone (sabb), and (4) going out to sit on the road waiting for a ruler.
The passage treats each separately.
2
State the Ruling and Its Basis
For each action, state whether it disqualifies the witness and why. Note that some actions are haram (e.g., lying insult, glorifying undeserving) while others remove muru'a (e.g., true insult to the face).
The hadith 'sabb al-muslim fisq' is the key proof for insult.
3
Analyze the Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf on Going Out
Khalaf says it nullifies 'adalah unless done for i'tibar (reflection). Ibn al-Shihnah and al-Fatawa al-Sughra add that the real cause is occupying the public road (shughl al-tariq). Evaluate which view is mufta bihi.
The author leans toward the occupation-of-road reasoning.
4
Apply the Custom Exception
The author notes that if a practice is widespread and not denounced, it might not disqualify. How does 'urf (custom) affect the ruling on going out?
This is a limiting principle.
5
Synthesize: A Witness's 'Adalah Checklist
From the passage, construct a list of conditions for a witness to be 'adil. Include prohibitions and exceptions (e.g., attending singing without fee, going out with proper intent).
This prepares for application.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الغناء
ولا تقبل شهادة من يجلس مجالس الغناء أو يتبع صوت المغنية ولا من يسمع وشهادة الشاعر ما لم يقذف في شعره مقبولة إلا إذا هجا ا هـ . وقد حرر ابن وهبان مسألة الشتم والخروج لقدوم الأمير تحريراً حسناً أحببت ذكره هنا الأولى قال : والفقه في ذلك الشتم لا يخلو إما أن يكون بما فيه أو بما ليس فيه في وجهه أو في غيبته، فإن كان بما ليس فيه فهو كذب وافتراء فيفسق به سواء كان في وجهه أو في غيبته وإن كان بما فيه في غيبته فهو غيبة وأنها توجب الفسق، وإن كان في وجهه ففيه إساءة أدب أنه من صنيع رعاعة الناس وسوقتهم الذين لا مروءة لهم ولا حياء فيهم وأن ذلك مما يسقط العدالة، وكذا إذا كان السب باللعنة والإبعاد مما يفعله من لا خلاق لهم من السوقة وغيرهم ومما يؤيد ذلك ما ورد في الحديث سباب المسلم فسوق وقتاله كفر (۱). قال ابن الأثير في النهاية: السب الشتم يقال سبه يسبه سباً وسباباً . قيل هذا محمول على من سبه أو قاتل مسلماً بغير تأويل، وقيل إنما قال ذلك على جهة التغليظ لا أنه يخرجه إلى الكفر والفسق وأقول : هذا خلاف الظاهر الثانية قال قاضيخان : إذا قدم الأمير بلدة فخرج الناس وجلسوا على الطريق ينتظرون قال خلف : بطلت عدالتهم إلا أن يذهبوا للاعتبار فحينئذ لا تبطل ا هـ. وحاصله أنها لا تبطل إلا إذا كان الأمير لا يصلح للتعظيم ولم يخرجوا للاعتبار، والفقه فيه أنهم إذا خرجوا لغير هذين الأمرين يكون طلوعهم من باب العبث واللعب وهو حرام، أو من أجل تعظيم من لا يستحق التعظيم وهو حرام أيضاً، والشخص إذا ارتكب حراماً قدح في عدالته وينبغي أن يكون ذلك على ما اعتاده أهل البلد فإن كان من عادة أهل البلد أنهم يفعلون ذلك ولا ينكرونه ولا يستخفونه فينبغي أن لا يقدح ا هـ.
وذكر العلامة ابن الشحنة بعده وفي واقعات عمر بن مازة تعليل عدم قبول شهادتهم بأن الطريق حق العامة فلم تعمل للجلوس فإذا جلس فقد شغل حق العامة فصار مرتكباً للحرام فسقطت عدالته. وفي الفتاوى الصغرى: لا تقبل شهادة من وقف على الطريق لأنه شغل الطريق، وهذا التعليل يفيد أن الخروج إذا تجرد عن شغل الطريق لا يكون قادحاً مطلقاً ولا ينافيه ما تقدم إذا تأملته فقول المصنف ينبغي إلى آخره» ليس كما ينبغي ا هـ . . وشرط في التهذيب لمنع شهادة المغني أن يأخذ جزاء عليه، ولتارك الجماعة أن يتركها مجاناً شهراً.
Singing: And the testimony of one who sits in gatherings of singing or follows the voice of a female singer is not accepted, nor of one who listens. And the testimony of a poet is accepted as long as he does not defame in his poetry, except if he satirizes. End quote. And Ibn Wahban has nicely analyzed the issue of insult and going out for the arrival of the ruler. I liked to mention it here. First opinion: He said: The fiqh in that: The insult is either with something in him or not in him, in his presence or absent. If it is with something not in him, it is lying and slander, so he becomes sinful whether in his presence or absent. If it is with something in him while he is absent, it is backbiting and that causes sin. If it is in his presence, it is bad manners, being the act of base and common people who have no muru'a and no modesty, and that it removes 'adalah. Likewise if the insult is by cursing and exiling, something done by those with no good share among the commoners and others. Supporting this is the hadith: 'Insulting a Muslim is transgression (fisq) and fighting him is disbelief (kufr).' Ibn al-Athir said in al-Nihayah: al-sabb is shatm; it is said he insulted him. It is said this is interpreted as one who insults or fights a Muslim without interpretation; it is also said that this is said as a hyperbole, not that it actually takes him out of Islam. And I say: this is contrary to the apparent. Second opinion: Qadi Khan said: When the ruler arrives in a town and people go out and sit on the road waiting, Khalaf said: their 'adalah is nullified unless they go for reflection (i'tibar), then it is not nullified. End quote. Its summary: it is not nullified unless the ruler is not worthy of glorification and they did not go for reflection. The fiqh in it: if they go out for other than these two matters, their going out is from the category of frivolity and play, which is haram, or for glorifying one who does not deserve glorification, also haram. A person who commits a sin is disqualified in his 'adalah. And it should be according to the custom of the people of the town: if it is the custom that they do that and do not denounce it nor hide it, then it should not disqualify. End quote. And the scholar Ibn al-Shihnah mentioned after this: In Waqi'at 'Umar ibn Mazah, the reason for not accepting their testimony is that the road is a public right; it was not made for sitting. So if one sits, he has occupied the public right, thus becomes a sinner, so his 'adalah falls. And in al-Fatawa al-Sughra: The testimony of one who stands on the road is not accepted because he occupies the road. This reasoning indicates that going out, if devoid of occupying the road, does not disqualify absolutely, and it does not contradict what preceded if you contemplate. So the author's statement 'it should be...' is not as it should be. End quote. And in al-Tahdhib, a condition for preventing the testimony of a singer is that he takes a fee for it, and for the one who leaves congregation that he leaves it without excuse for a month.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition on several causes of jarh. First, singing: attending singing gatherings or following a female singer's voice disqualifies a witness, as does listening (with apparent intent). The poet's testimony is accepted unless his poetry involves defamation (qadhf) or satire (hija). Ibn Wahban then analyzes insult: if it's false (lying), it's fisq; if true but behind the person's back, it's ghibah (backbiting); if true but to the face, it's bad manners and removes muru'a. The hadith 'sabb al-muslim fisq' supports this, though some interpret it as hyperbole. Next, going out to see the ruler: Qadikhan reports from Khalaf that it nullifies 'adalah unless done for reflection (i'tibar). The reasoning is that it involves either frivolous play (haram) or glorifying the undeserving (also haram). However, custom ('urf) of the town may mitigate this. Ibn al-Shihnah and al-Fatawa al-Sughra add that occupying the road (shughl al-tariq) is the real cause. The author then notes that in al-Tahdhib, for a singer, taking a fee is a condition for disqualification. Thus, not all singing or going out necessarily disqualifies; context matters.

Key Terms
الغيبة (al-ghibah): Speaking about a person in their absence with something true but disliked.
المروءة (al-muru'a): Moral propriety and decency expected of a respectable person.
الشتم (al-shatm): Insulting or reviling someone.
السب (al-sabb): Cursing or verbal abuse.
الفسق (al-fisq): Major sin or persistent minor sin that removes 'adalah.
الاعتبار (al-i'tibar): Reflection, taking a lesson; a valid reason for going out to see a ruler.
شغل الطريق (shughl al-tariq): Occupying the public road without right.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Wahban's analysis, what is the ruling if a person insults another with a false statement (lie) in his absence?
A) It is backbiting (ghibah) and disqualifies.
B) It is lying and slander (fisq) and disqualifies.
C) It is bad manners but does not affect testimony.
D) It is permissible if the person is a sinner.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a witness's 'adalah is lost through specific haram acts (insult, some singing, frivolous waiting) or loss of muru'a, but context (intent, custom, occupation of road) can limit the disqualification.

Rejected Testimony in Suspect Contexts
Rulings on testimony from specific contexts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on testimony of inmates, children at play, and women in baths, and explain the underlying cause of tuhma.

Core Idea
Testimony from inmates, children, and women in baths is automatically rejected due to inherent bias or frivolity.

The Hanafi school identifies three contexts where testimony is not accepted due to embedded suspicion: prison inmates testifying about prison events, children testifying about playground incidents, and women testifying about bathhouse incidents. This lesson explains the rulings and their rationale.

Guided Path
1
List the three categories
Identify the groups: prison inmates, children at play, and women in baths.
These are situations where tuhma (suspicion of partiality or frivolity) is presumed.
2
State the ruling for each
The passage explicitly says 'la tuqbal' (not accepted). For inmates and children, it is absolute; for women, it is not accepted even if need requires.
The phrase 'wa in massat al-hajah' indicates urgency does not override the defect.
3
Identify the underlying cause
The common cause is that these settings create a strong presumption of bias or lack of seriousness, making testimony unreliable.
This is based on the principle that 'adalah requires freedom from tuhma.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي خزانة الفتاوى : إذا قدم الأمير بلدة فخرج الناس وجلسوا في الطريق ونظروا إليه قال خلف بطلت شهادتهم إلا أن يذهبوا للاعتبار والفتوى أنهم إذا خرجوا لتعظيم من لا يستحق التعظيم لا للاعتبار تبطل عدالتهم ولا تقبل شهادة أهل السجن بعضهم على بعض فيما يقع في السجن، وكذا شهادة الصبيان بعضهم على بعض فيما يقع في الملاعب، ومنها شهادة النساء فيما يقع في الحمامات لا تقبل وإن مست الحاجة ا هـ. وذكر ابن وهبان معزياً إلى شرح أدب القضاء للحسام الشهيد : لا تقبل شهادة الأشراف من أهل العراق لأنهم قوم يتعصبون فإذا نابت أحداً منهم نائبة أتى سيد قومه فيشفع فلا يؤمن أن يشهد له بزور ا هـ . كل متعصب لا تقبل شهادته وفي المجتبى : من أكل فوق الشبع سقطت عدالته عند الأكثر
وعلى هذا
قوله : ( فقول المصنف ينبغي الخ) أي قول ابن وهبان وينبغي أن يكون ذلك على ما اعتاده أهل البلد الخ قال الرملي : فتحرر من مجموع ما ذكر أنه إن كان الأمير غير صالح قدح في العدالة، وإن كان صالحاً ولم يشغل الطريق لا يقدح، وإن شغله قدح وأنت على علم بأن الحكم يدور مع
العلة
فائدة: شاهد تظهر عليه كرامة مع فسقه هل تقبل شهادته أم لا؟ الظاهر لا. وقد سئل ابن حجر الهيتمي الشافعي عنها فأجاب بقوله : لا تقبل فقد قال الشافعي رحمه الله : لو رأيت صاحب . بدعة يطير في الهواء لم أقبله حتى يتوب عن بدعته ؛ ذكره أبو نعيم قد تظهر الكرامة على يد فاسق بل کافر کالسامري فإنه رأى فرس جبريل عليه السلام حتى أخذ من تراب حافرها وجعله في العجل فخار. ونقل ابن العماد عن الشيخ أبي محمد النيسابوري أنه قال : يجب على الولي إخفاء الكرامة اهـ . ولا شيء من قواعدنا يأباه قوله : (وعلى هذا كل متعصب لا تقبل شهادته) قال الرملي قال الغزي قلت : وفي الخلاصة من كتاب القضاء : فإن عدله اثنان وجرحه اثنان فالجرح أولى إلا إذا كان بينهم
And in Khizanat al-Fatawa: If the ruler arrives in a town and people go out and sit on the road and look at him, Khalaf said: their testimony is nullified unless they go for reflection. The fatwa is that if they go out to glorify one who does not deserve glorification, not for reflection, their 'adalah is nullified. And the testimony of prison inmates against each other regarding what occurs in prison is not accepted, likewise the testimony of children against each other regarding what occurs in playgrounds, and among them the testimony of women regarding what occurs in baths is not accepted even if need requires it. End quote. And Ibn Wahban mentioned, attributing to Sharh Adab al-Qada' of al-Husam al-Shahid: The testimony of the nobles of the people of Iraq is not accepted because they are a partisan people; if a mishap befalls one of them, he goes to the leader of his people for intercession, so it is not safe that he testify falsely for him. End quote. Every partisan person's testimony is not accepted. And in al-Mujtaba: Whoever eats beyond satiation, his 'adalah falls according to most scholars. And upon this: His statement: 'so the author's statement 'it should be...'' meaning Ibn Wahban's statement 'it should be according to the custom of the people of the town...' Al-Ramli said: It is established from the sum of what was mentioned that if the ruler is not righteous, it discredits 'adalah; if he is righteous and did not occupy the road, it does not discredit; if he occupies the road, it discredits. And you know that the ruling revolves with the cause. Benefit: A witness upon whom a miracle (karamah) appears while he is sinful, is his testimony accepted or not? Apparently not. Ibn Hajar al-Haytami al-Shafi'i was asked about it, and he answered: It is not accepted. Al-Shafi'i said: If I see an innovator flying in the air, I would not accept him until he repents from his innovation. Abu Nu'aym mentioned it. Indeed, a miracle may appear at the hands of a sinful person, even an unbeliever, like al-Samiri who saw the horse of Jibril (peace be upon him) and took dust from its hoof and placed it in the calf, and it lowed. And Ibn al-'Imad transmitted from al-Shaykh Abu Muhammad al-Naysaburi that he said: It is obligatory upon the saint to conceal karamah. End quote. And nothing from our principles prevents it. His statement: 'And upon this, every partisan person's testimony is not accepted.' Al-Ramli said, al-Ghazzi said: In al-Khulasah from the Book of Qada: If two declare him just and two declare him defective, the defect takes precedence unless there is between them...
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage concludes the chapter with several specific rules. First, from Khizanat al-Fatawa: testimony of prison inmates against each other is not accepted due to likely partiality. Similarly, children in playgrounds and women in baths are not accepted because these settings often involve lack of seriousness or potential bias. Then Ibn Wahban quotes Sharh Adab al-Qada': the testimony of Iraqi nobles is not accepted because they are partisan (muta'assib) — they might testify falsely out of tribal loyalty. This leads to the general rule: every partisan person's testimony is rejected. Al-Mujtaba adds that overeating to the point of satiation also removes 'adalah (as it indicates lack of self-control). Then Ramli reconciles the earlier discussions on going out for the ruler: the cause is either the ruler's unworthiness or occupation of the road. Finally, a benefit: even if a sinful person shows a karamah (miracle), his testimony is not accepted, following a statement by al-Shafi'i (though the Hanafis agree based on their own principles). The section ends with a note that in cases of conflicting testimony, jarh (discrediting) outweighs ta'dil (crediting).

Key Terms
التعصب (al-ta'assub): Blind partisanship or group loyalty that may lead to false testimony.
الكرامة (al-karamah): Supernatural event performed by a saint (wali) as a sign of divine favor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is accepted if the women are 'adl.
B) It is not accepted even if necessity requires it.
C) It is accepted only if no male witnesses exist.
D) It is accepted if the women testify outside the bath.

Key Takeaway
Testimony from inmates, children at play, and women in baths is not accepted due to the inherent suspicion of bias or frivolity in those contexts.

Partisanship and Overeating as Defects in 'Adalah
Rulings on partisanship and overeating as defects in 'adalah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify partisanship (ta'assub) and overeating as defects in 'adalah that disqualify testimony, and apply the rule that jarh takes precedence over ta'dil.

Core Idea
Partisanship and gluttony reveal weak character, making testimony unreliable.

The Hanafi school identifies partisanship (al-ta'assub) and overeating beyond satiation as defects in 'adalah. Partisanship leads to potential bias, while overeating indicates lack of self-control. These defects make a witness's testimony unacceptable. The lesson also covers that in case of conflicting testimony about a witness's character, the negative assessment (jarh) is given priority.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two defects
Partisanship (ta'assub) and overeating (al-akl fawqa al-shib') are explicitly mentioned as causes for rejecting testimony.
Both relate to moral or psychological flaws.
2
State the ruling for each
The passage states: 'Every partisan person's testimony is not accepted' and 'Whoever eats beyond satiation, his 'adalah falls according to most scholars.'
The overeating ruling is from al-Mujtaba and is not unanimous but accepted by 'most'.
3
Explain the underlying cause
Partisanship creates bias and potential false testimony to support one's group. Overeating indicates lack of self-control and thus lack of reliability.
Both are considered forms of fisq or deficiency in moral character.
4
Note the rule on jarh vs ta'dil
When witnesses disagree about a person's 'adalah (two declare him just, two declare him defective), the jarh (defect) takes precedence because it reveals hidden information.
This is from al-Khulasah cited at the end of the passage.

وفي خزانة الفتاوى : إذا قدم الأمير بلدة فخرج الناس وجلسوا في الطريق ونظروا إليه قال خلف بطلت شهادتهم إلا أن يذهبوا للاعتبار والفتوى أنهم إذا خرجوا لتعظيم من لا يستحق التعظيم لا للاعتبار تبطل عدالتهم ولا تقبل شهادة أهل السجن بعضهم على بعض فيما يقع في السجن، وكذا شهادة الصبيان بعضهم على بعض فيما يقع في الملاعب، ومنها شهادة النساء فيما يقع في الحمامات لا تقبل وإن مست الحاجة ا هـ. وذكر ابن وهبان معزياً إلى شرح أدب القضاء للحسام الشهيد : لا تقبل شهادة الأشراف من أهل العراق لأنهم قوم يتعصبون فإذا نابت أحداً منهم نائبة أتى سيد قومه فيشفع فلا يؤمن أن يشهد له بزور ا هـ . كل متعصب لا تقبل شهادته وفي المجتبى : من أكل فوق الشبع سقطت عدالته عند الأكثر
وعلى هذا
قوله : ( فقول المصنف ينبغي الخ) أي قول ابن وهبان وينبغي أن يكون ذلك على ما اعتاده أهل البلد الخ قال الرملي : فتحرر من مجموع ما ذكر أنه إن كان الأمير غير صالح قدح في العدالة، وإن كان صالحاً ولم يشغل الطريق لا يقدح، وإن شغله قدح وأنت على علم بأن الحكم يدور مع
العلة
فائدة: شاهد تظهر عليه كرامة مع فسقه هل تقبل شهادته أم لا؟ الظاهر لا. وقد سئل ابن حجر الهيتمي الشافعي عنها فأجاب بقوله : لا تقبل فقد قال الشافعي رحمه الله : لو رأيت صاحب . بدعة يطير في الهواء لم أقبله حتى يتوب عن بدعته ؛ ذكره أبو نعيم قد تظهر الكرامة على يد فاسق بل کافر کالسامري فإنه رأى فرس جبريل عليه السلام حتى أخذ من تراب حافرها وجعله في العجل فخار. ونقل ابن العماد عن الشيخ أبي محمد النيسابوري أنه قال : يجب على الولي إخفاء الكرامة اهـ . ولا شيء من قواعدنا يأباه قوله : (وعلى هذا كل متعصب لا تقبل شهادته) قال الرملي قال الغزي قلت : وفي الخلاصة من كتاب القضاء : فإن عدله اثنان وجرحه اثنان فالجرح أولى إلا إذا كان بينهم
And in Khizanat al-Fatawa: If the ruler arrives in a town and people go out and sit on the road and look at him, Khalaf said: their testimony is nullified unless they go for reflection. The fatwa is that if they go out to glorify one who does not deserve glorification, not for reflection, their 'adalah is nullified. And the testimony of prison inmates against each other regarding what occurs in prison is not accepted, likewise the testimony of children against each other regarding what occurs in playgrounds, and among them the testimony of women regarding what occurs in baths is not accepted even if need requires it. End quote. And Ibn Wahban mentioned, attributing to Sharh Adab al-Qada' of al-Husam al-Shahid: The testimony of the nobles of the people of Iraq is not accepted because they are a partisan people; if a mishap befalls one of them, he goes to the leader of his people for intercession, so it is not safe that he testify falsely for him. End quote. Every partisan person's testimony is not accepted. And in al-Mujtaba: Whoever eats beyond satiation, his 'adalah falls according to most scholars. And upon this: His statement: 'so the author's statement 'it should be...'' meaning Ibn Wahban's statement 'it should be according to the custom of the people of the town...' Al-Ramli said: It is established from the sum of what was mentioned that if the ruler is not righteous, it discredits 'adalah; if he is righteous and did not occupy the road, it does not discredit; if he occupies the road, it discredits. And you know that the ruling revolves with the cause. Benefit: A witness upon whom a miracle (karamah) appears while he is sinful, is his testimony accepted or not? Apparently not. Ibn Hajar al-Haytami al-Shafi'i was asked about it, and he answered: It is not accepted. Al-Shafi'i said: If I see an innovator flying in the air, I would not accept him until he repents from his innovation. Abu Nu'aym mentioned it. Indeed, a miracle may appear at the hands of a sinful person, even an unbeliever, like al-Samiri who saw the horse of Jibril (peace be upon him) and took dust from its hoof and placed it in the calf, and it lowed. And Ibn al-'Imad transmitted from al-Shaykh Abu Muhammad al-Naysaburi that he said: It is obligatory upon the saint to conceal karamah. End quote. And nothing from our principles prevents it. His statement: 'And upon this, every partisan person's testimony is not accepted.' Al-Ramli said, al-Ghazzi said: In al-Khulasah from the Book of Qada: If two declare him just and two declare him defective, the defect takes precedence unless there is between them...
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage concludes the chapter with several specific rules. First, from Khizanat al-Fatawa: testimony of prison inmates against each other is not accepted due to likely partiality. Similarly, children in playgrounds and women in baths are not accepted because these settings often involve lack of seriousness or potential bias. Then Ibn Wahban quotes Sharh Adab al-Qada': the testimony of Iraqi nobles is not accepted because they are partisan (muta'assib) — they might testify falsely out of tribal loyalty. This leads to the general rule: every partisan person's testimony is rejected. Al-Mujtaba adds that overeating to the point of satiation also removes 'adalah (as it indicates lack of self-control). Then Ramli reconciles the earlier discussions on going out for the ruler: the cause is either the ruler's unworthiness or occupation of the road. Finally, a benefit: even if a sinful person shows a karamah (miracle), his testimony is not accepted, following a statement by al-Shafi'i (though the Hanafis agree based on their own principles). The section ends with a note that in cases of conflicting testimony, jarh (discrediting) outweighs ta'dil (crediting).

Key Terms
التعصب (al-ta'assub): Blind partisanship or group loyalty that may lead to false testimony.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on the testimony of a person who is a known partisan (muta'assib)?
A) It is accepted if he is otherwise truthful.
B) It is not accepted because partisanship is a defect in 'adalah.
C) It is accepted only if he testifies against his own group.
D) It is left to the judge's discretion based on the case.

Key Takeaway
Partisanship and overeating are specific defects that nullify a witness's 'adalah, making testimony unacceptable; and jarh (defect) takes precedence over ta'dil (crediting).

Miracles (Karamah) Do Not Override Sinfulness for Testimony
Benefit: karamah does not render a sinful witness acceptable

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain that the appearance of a miracle (karamah) on a sinful person does not make his testimony acceptable, and state the Shafi'i and Hanafi bases for this ruling.

Core Idea
A miracle does not erase sin; character alone determines testimony's acceptance.

A special benefit (fa'idah) in the passage addresses a nuanced question: if a sinful witness shows supernatural signs (karamah), does that restore his 'adalah? The answer is no, supported by a strong statement from Imam al-Shafi'i and accepted by Hanafi principles. This lesson clarifies that miracles do not override fisq.

Guided Path
1
Identify the question
The text asks: Can a witness who is sinful (fasiq) but shows karamah have his testimony accepted?
Karamah is a supernatural event occurring through a saint; but here the person is sinful, so it's contradictory.
2
State the answer
The answer is clearly no: 'al-zahir la' (apparently not).
This is supported by a quote from al-Shafi'i: even if an innovator flies in the air, he is not accepted until he repents.
3
Provide the rationale
Miracles can appear through sinful people and even disbelievers (like al-Samiri). They are not a sign of 'adalah. 'Adalah depends on religious and moral rectitude, not supernatural events.
The story of al-Samiri making the golden calf is cited as proof that karamah can occur from kuffar.
4
Note the additional guideline
The passage also quotes that it is obligatory upon the saint to conceal his karamah, highlighting that miracles are not meant to be public credentials.
This confirms that karamah is not a tool for establishing credibility.

Shahadat — Testimony

Grounds for Rejecting Testimony: Violating Public Rights and Moral Probity
Expansion on grounds for rejecting testimony

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which actions void 'adālah according to the Hanafi school and apply the distinction between occupying public right and mere presence to determine whether testimony is admissible.

Core Idea
Occupying public right without need is ḥarām and voids 'adālah; mere presence without occupation does not.

The requirement of 'adālah (moral probity) for witnesses is a cornerstone of Islamic judiciary. This passage from al-Bahr al-Rā'iq, a major Hanafi commentary, lists several actions that compromise probity—from sitting on the public road to attending baths—and explains the legal reasoning behind each rejection. Understanding these grounds helps us appreciate how the Hanafi school safeguards the integrity of testimony by screening out those whose actions show disregard for public rights or indicate unreliability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Core Principle
Read the passage carefully. The first case (sitting on the road) is explained with a clear rationale: the road is a public right, so occupying it without need is a ḥarām act, which voids 'adālah. This principle applies to all other cases: any ḥarām act that indicates moral laxity or unreliability leads to rejection of testimony.
2
Distinguish Between Occupation and Mere Presence
The passage clarifies that simply exiting the house or standing on the road is not automatically impeaching. The key is whether the person actually occupies or obstructs the public right. This distinction resolves apparent contradictions between different Hanafi sources.
The fatwā favors the view that only actual occupation voids 'adālah.
3
Examine Additional Cases for Common Thread
The passage then lists four more situations: watching an emir for undue honor, testimony of prisoners about each other, children's testimony about playground incidents, and women's testimony about events in baths. Identify what each has in common: they all involve a context that is prone to bias (prison), frivolity (children), or inherent unreliability (baths due to lack of modesty).
The underlying concern is the same: such testimony is not trustworthy.
4
Apply the Distinction to New Scenarios
Take the example of watching an emir: if people go out to honor an undeserving ruler, that is ḥarām and voids 'adālah. But if they go for legitimate reflection (i'tibār), it does not. Apply this same logic to other situations—does sitting on the road necessarily involve occupation? Only if it actually impedes public use.
5
Synthesize the Ruling
Now state the overall Hanafi position: testimony is rejected when the witness commits a ḥarām act related to the testimony context, or when the context itself is inherently unreliable (prison, playgrounds, baths). The burden is on the judge to assess each case based on these guidelines.
The muftā bihi (operative ruling) is that only actual occupation of the road is impeaching.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذكر العلامة ابن الشحنة بعده وفي واقعات عمر بن مازة تعليل عدم قبول شهادتهم بأن الطريق حق العامة فلم تعمل للجلوس فإذا جلس فقد شغل حق العامة فصار مرتكباً للحرام فسقطت عدالته. وفي الفتاوى الصغرى: لا تقبل شهادة من وقف على الطريق لأنه شغل الطريق، وهذا التعليل يفيد أن الخروج إذا تجرد عن شغل الطريق لا يكون قادحاً مطلقاً ولا ينافيه ما تقدم إذا تأملته فقول المصنف ينبغي إلى آخره» ليس كما ينبغي ا هـ . . وشرط في التهذيب لمنع شهادة المغني أن يأخذ جزاء عليه، ولتارك الجماعة أن يتركها مجاناً شهراً. وفي خزانة الفتاوى : إذا قدم الأمير بلدة فخرج الناس وجلسوا في الطريق ونظروا إليه قال خلف بطلت شهادتهم إلا أن يذهبوا للاعتبار والفتوى أنهم إذا خرجوا لتعظيم من لا يستحق التعظيم لا للاعتبار تبطل عدالتهم ولا تقبل شهادة أهل السجن بعضهم على بعض فيما يقع في السجن، وكذا شهادة الصبيان بعضهم على بعض فيما يقع في الملاعب، ومنها شهادة النساء فيما يقع في الحمامات لا تقبل وإن مست الحاجة ا هـ.
And the scholar Ibn al-Shihnah mentioned after it, and in the Wāqi‘āt of ‘Umar ibn Māzah, an explanation for the non-acceptance of their testimony: because the road is a public right, so it was not made for sitting. So when one sits, he has occupied a public right, thus becomes a committer of the unlawful, so his probity falls. And in al-Fatāwā al-Ṣughrā: The testimony of one who stands on the road is not accepted because he occupies the road. This explanation indicates that if exiting the house is free from occupying the road, it is not absolutely impeaching, and it does not contradict what preceded if you reflect. So the author's saying 'it should be...' is not as it should be. End. And a condition was stated in al-Tahdhīb for preventing the testimony of a singer: that he takes payment for it, and for the one who abandons the congregation: that he abandons it for a month without excuse. And in Khizānat al-Fatāwā: When an emir arrives in a town, and people go out, sit on the road, and look at him—Khalaf said: their testimony becomes void, unless they go for reflection. And the fatwā is that if they go out to honor one who does not deserve honor, not for reflection, their probity becomes void. And the testimony of prison inmates about one another regarding what happens in prison is not accepted, nor is the testimony of children about one another regarding what happens in playgrounds, and among them is the testimony of women regarding what happens in baths: it is not accepted, even if there is need. End.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement with explanation from multiple Hanafi authorities. It addresses the rejection of testimony based on actions that compromise 'adālah (moral probity). The primary issue is sitting on the public road, which is a violation of public right and thus a ḥarām act, leading to loss of 'adālah. The commentary distinguishes between sitting to occupy the road (which is a clear violation) and simply exiting without occupying (which may not be impeaching). The condition of accepting payment for singing or abandoning the congregation for a month without excuse are also grounds for rejecting testimony. The case of the emir's arrival specifies that if people go out to honor an undeserving ruler, it is ḥarām and voids testimony; if for legitimate reflection, it does not. Additionally, the testimony of prisoners about each other during incarceration, children about each other in playgrounds, and women about events in baths are rejected categorically, even if needed. This is based on the principle that such situations are prone to bias, frivolity, or lack of reliability. The authority_weight is fatawa_mutakhkhirin from sources like Khizānat al-Fatāwā, though the principle of ḥarām acts voiding 'adālah is from Zahir al-Riwayah.

Key Terms
عدالة ('adālah): Legal probity; a witness must be 'adl—upright, avoiding major sins and not persisting in minor sins.
شغل الطريق (shughl al-ṭarīq): Occupying the public road without legitimate need, which is a violation of public right.
الفتوى (al-fatwā): The operative ruling (muftā bihi) in a given school.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the reason for rejecting the testimony of a person who sits on the public road?
A) Sitting on the road is always a major sin.
B) They have occupied a public right, thus committed a ḥarām act.
C) The road is only for walking, not sitting.
D) They have wasted time.

Key Takeaway
Testimony is rejected when the witness's actions show disregard for public rights or indicate unreliability, but the ruling hinges on whether the act actually constitutes a sin or a context of bias.

Shahadat — Testimony

Partisanship and Gluttony as Grounds for Rejecting Testimony
Ruling statement on partisanship and gluttony voiding 'adālah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on rejecting testimony due to partisanship and identify the conditions under which a partisan's jarḥ is unacceptable.

Core Idea
Partisanship voids probity, making testimony and jarḥ from a biased witness rejected.

In this lesson, we examine a classical Hanafi ruling that partisanship—hatred based on tribe, party, or association—destroys a witness's probity ('adālah). The passage also connects gluttony to loss of self-control, further weakening credibility. Understanding this principle is crucial for evaluating witness reliability in Islamic judiciary.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ground for rejection
Locate the specific defects mentioned: partisanship (ta‘aṣṣub) and eating above satiety. Note that both are said to void 'adālah.
Partisanship includes hatred due to tribe, party, companions, or relatives.
2
Understand the evidentiary implication
If a witness is partisan, their testimony is not accepted. Moreover, if two witnesses give jarḥ (discrediting) against a person, but the testifiers are partisan, their jarḥ is also rejected. This is because their own testimony would be inadmissible.
The principle: 'the original testimony is not accepted in case of partisanship, so the jarḥ is even more unsuitable.'
3
Differentiate from normal balancing
Normally, if ta‘dīl and jarḥ are equal, ta‘dīl is given precedence. But partisanship shifts the rule: the jarḥ is rejected entirely.
This is an exception to the general rule of preferring ta‘dīl.
4
Connect to broader Hanafi principles
The ruling reflects the Hanafi view that major sins (kabā'ir) void 'adālah. Bias and lack of self-control are considered major moral failings.
Authority sources: al-Mujtabā, al-Khulāṣah, and later authorities like al-Ramlī and al-Ghazzī.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذكر ابن وهبان معزياً إلى شرح أدب القضاء للحسام الشهيد : لا تقبل شهادة الأشراف من أهل العراق لأنهم قوم يتعصبون فإذا نابت أحداً منهم نائبة أتى سيد قومه فيشفع فلا يؤمن أن يشهد له بزور ا هـ . كل متعصب لا تقبل شهادته وفي المجتبى : من أكل فوق الشبع سقطت عدالته عند الأكثر وعلى هذا كل متعصب لا تقبل شهادته قال الرملي قال الغزي قلت : وفي الخلاصة من كتاب القضاء : فإن عدله اثنان وجرحه اثنان فالجرح أولى إلا إذا كان بينهم تعصب فإنه لا يقبل جرحهم لأن أصل الشهادة لا تقبل عند العصبية فالجرح أولى اهـ. وفي معين الحكام في موانع قبول الشهادة قال : ومنه العصبية وهو أن يبغض الرجل الرجل لأنه من بني أو من قبيلة كذا .اهـ. أقول من التعصب أن يبغضه لأنه من حزب فلان أو من أصحابه أو من أقاربه
And Ibn Wahbān mentioned, attributing to Sharḥ Adab al-Qaḍā’ of al-Ḥusām al-Shahīd: The testimony of the nobles of Iraq is not accepted because they are a partisan people. When a calamity befalls one of them, he goes to the chief of his people who intercedes, so it is not safe that he testifies falsely for him. End. Every partisan's testimony is not accepted. And in al-Mujtabā: Whoever eats above satiety, his probity is void according to the majority. And upon this, every partisan's testimony is not accepted. Al-Ramlī said: Al-Ghazzī said: I say: And in al-Khulāṣah from the book of judiciary: If two declare him just and two declare him a liar, the ta‘dīl is given precedence, except if there is partisanship between them, then their jarḥ is not accepted, because the original testimony is not accepted in case of partisanship, so the jarḥ is even more [unacceptable]. End. And in Mu‘īn al-Ḥukkām regarding obstacles to accepting testimony, he said: Among them is partisanship, which is that a man hates another because he is from Banī so-and-so or from such-and-such tribe. End. I say: Partisanship includes hating him because he is from the party of so-and-so or from his companions or from his relatives.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement with multiple authorities on partisanship (ta‘aṣṣub) as a ground for rejecting testimony. It begins with a quote from Ibn Wahbān that the testimony of the nobles of Iraq is rejected due to their partisanship, as intercession can lead to false testimony. Then the general principle: 'Every partisan's testimony is not accepted.' This is supported by a quote from al-Mujtabā that eating above satiety voids probity according to most scholars, linking overindulgence to lack of self-control. The commentary then discusses the scenario of equal ta‘dīl and jarḥ: normally ta‘dīl is given precedence, but if the witnesses are partial, their jarḥ is rejected because their own testimony would be rejected due to partisanship. The passage concludes by defining partisanship as hatred based on tribe, party, companions, or relatives. This is a ruling_statement with no explicit scriptural evidence; it relies on the principle that any act indicating moral depravity or bias voids ‘adālah. The authority_weight includes both Zahir al-Riwayah principles (via al-Mujtabā) and later fatawa (via Ibn Wahbān).

Key Terms
عصبية (‘aṣabiyyah): Partisanship; hatred of a person due to his tribe, party, or association, leading to bias.
عدالة (‘adālah): Probity; the moral integrity required for a witness's testimony to be accepted.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The jarḥ is given precedence.
B) The ta‘dīl is given precedence.
C) Neither is accepted.
D) The jarḥ is rejected because the testifiers are partisan.

Key Takeaway
Partisanship and gluttony are considered major sins that void a witness's probity, leading to rejection of their testimony and any discrediting statements they make.

Testimony of a Fasiq Showing a Karamah
Fatwa-style benefit on karamah and testimony

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that a fasiq's testimony is rejected even if he exhibits a karamah, and identify the Shafi'i proof used to support this ruling.

Core Idea
A karamah does not restore a fasiq’s credibility; fisq voids ‘adālah regardless.

Imagine a witness known for sinful behavior suddenly performs an apparent miracle. Does that make him reliable? This lesson tackles a subtle question: can a fasiq’s testimony be accepted if he shows a karamah? Drawing on a famous Shafi‘i principle and the Quranic example of al-Sāmirī, the Hanafi school affirms that miracles do not erase sin. Testimony requires probity (‘adālah), and fisq destroys it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the legal question
The act is giving testimony by a witness who is a fasiq (sinner) but displays a karamah (supernatural sign). The question is whether the karamah restores the witness's credibility.
The passage treats this as a judicial ruling for the judge.
2
State the ruling
The testimony is not accepted. The ruling is general: any fasiq’s testimony is rejected, even if he shows a karamah.
The ruling is a straightforward application of the principle that ‘adālah requires avoidance of major sins.
3
Identify the proof (dalil)
The proof is a statement attributed to Imam al-Shāfi‘ī: even if an innovator flies in the air, he is not accepted until he repents. This is a conditional that prioritizes moral probity over extraordinary events.
Although the proof is from Shafi‘i, the Hanafis accept its logic. The example of al-Sāmirī (a disbeliever who produced a miraculous calf) further shows that karāmāt can occur without moral goodness.
4
Note the thamarah al-khilaf
Some might mistakenly think a karamah indicates divine favor implying probity. This lesson clarifies that karāmāt are not evidence of ‘adālah; the ruling remains the same across all schools.
The cross-madhhab status is muttafaq alayh (agreed upon).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فائدة: شاهد تظهر عليه كرامة مع فسقه هل تقبل شهادته أم لا؟ الظاهر لا. وقد سئل ابن حجر الهيتمي الشافعي عنها فأجاب بقوله : لا تقبل فقد قال الشافعي رحمه الله : لو رأيت صاحب بدعة يطير في الهواء لم أقبله حتى يتوب عن بدعته ؛ ذكره أبو نعيم قد تظهر الكرامة على يد فاسق بل کافر کالسامري فإنه رأى فرس جبريل عليه السلام حتى أخذ من تراب حافرها وجعله في العجل فخار. ونقل ابن العماد عن الشيخ أبي محمد النيسابوري أنه قال : يجب على الولي إخفاء الكرامة اهـ . ولا شيء من قواعدنا يأباه
Benefit: A witness upon whom a karamah appears while he is a fasiq—is his testimony accepted or not? Apparently not. And Ibn Ḥajar al-Haythamī al-Shāfi‘ī was asked about it, and he answered: It is not accepted, for al-Shāfi‘ī said: If I saw a practitioner of innovation flying in the air, I would not accept him until he repents from his innovation. Abū Nu‘aym mentioned it. A karamah may appear at the hands of a fasiq, even a disbeliever, like al-Sāmirī, for he saw the horse of Jibrīl, peace be upon him, until he took dust from its hoof and put it in the calf, and it bellows. And Ibn al-‘Imād transmitted from Shaykh Abū Muḥammad al-Naysābūrī that he said: It is obligatory upon the saint to conceal the karamah. End. And nothing from our principles rejects it.
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage is a fatwa-style benefit (fā'idah) addressing a hypothetical: if a fasiq (sinner) witness exhibits a karamah (miraculous sign), does that rehabilitate his testimony? The answer is no. The passage relies on a famous principle from Imam al-Shāfi‘ī that even extraordinary signs do not restore credibility if the person is sinful or an innovator. The example of al-Sāmirī (the Samaritan) in the Quran is cited: he was a disbeliever yet possessed supernatural ability. Therefore, karamah is not proof of probity. The Hanafi position aligns with this: testimony requires ‘adālah, and sinfulness voids it regardless of any miraculous occurrence. The authority_weight is from Shafi‘i source (Ibn Ḥajar) but adopted in Hanafi fatawa; the principle that karamah does not affect testimony is consistent with Hanafi usul.

Key Terms
كرامة (karāmah): A supernatural event manifesting through a non-prophet, considered a sign of divine favor but not necessarily of moral probity.
فسق (fisq): Sinfulness; committing major sins or persisting in minor sins, which voids legal probity (‘adālah).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the testimony of a fasiq who shows a karamah rejected?
A) Because karamah only appears for prophets.
B) Because a fasiq cannot show a karamah.
C) Because fisq (sinfulness) voids ‘adālah regardless of any miracle.
D) Because the witness must first hide the karamah.

Key Takeaway
A fasiq's testimony remains rejected even if he displays a karamah, because probity (‘adālah) requires moral uprightness, not miraculous signs.

Rejecting Testimony for Immodesty in Bath and Public
Lack of modesty in bath and public as grounds for rejecting testimony

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two actions that lead to rejection of testimony and explain the Hanafi reasoning linking them to lack of ‘adālah.

Core Idea
Public immodesty—whether ḥarām (exposing ‘awrah) or merely undignified (tark al-murū’ah)—voids a witness’s credibility.

Testimony in Islamic courts requires the witness to be ‘ādil (just). But what actions destroy that justice? This passage from al-Bahr al-Rā’iq gives two concrete examples: entering a bath without a waist-wrapper (izār) and walking in public wearing only trousers (sirwāl). The proof combines a direct prohibition from Abū Ḥanīfah and the principle of murū’ah. Let’s see how these acts affect testimony and why.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two prohibited acts
Find in the passage the two specific actions that make testimony rejected. Note that one is directly ḥarām and the other is considered a lack of murū’ah.
The first is entering the bath without izār; the second is walking in the road wearing only sirwāl.
2
Locate the proof for each
For the first action, the passage quotes Abū Ḥanīfah’s statement. For the second, it cites al-Karkhī’s fatwa. Identify these as the textual evidence.
Abū Ḥanīfah’s words are a poetic prohibition; al-Karkhī gives a reasoned ruling.
3
Analyse the legal reasoning
Why does each action void ‘adālah? The first is a clear sin (kashf al-‘awrah). The second is ‘tark al-murū’ah’—abandoning social decency. Both indicate a flawed character in Hanafi eyes.
Murū’ah is not a sin per se but a requirement of being a trustworthy witness.
4
Note the ikhtilāf
The passage hints that some scholars may differ on the second ground. Recognise that the Hanafi school is strict on public decorum, but not all madhāhib agree.
The common misunderstanding is that wearing only trousers is always disqualifying; it depends on local custom and context.
5
Apply to a new scenario
Consider: does the same reasoning apply if the immodest act happens in private? The passage discusses public behaviour (bath, road). Private acts may not affect testimony because they do not damage one’s reputation in the community.
The key factor is publicity and the impact on the witness’s moral standing in society.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (أو يدخل الحمام بغير إزار) لأن كشف العورة حرام ورأى أبو حنيفة رجلاً في الحمام بغير إزار فقال : ألا يا عباد الله خافوا إلهكم ولا تدخلوا الحمام من غير مئزر . وعلى هذا فرعوا كما قدمناه عدم قبول شهادة النساء في الحمامات. وذكر الكرخي أن من يمشي في الطريق بالسراويل وحده ليس عليه غيره لا تقبل شهادته لأنه تارك للمروءة ا هـ
His saying: 'Or he enters the bath without an izār'—because exposing the ‘awrah is ḥarām. And Abū Ḥanīfah saw a man in the bath without an izār and said: 'O servants of Allah, fear your Allah, and do not enter the bath without a waist-wrapper.' And upon this they derived, as we have presented, the non-acceptance of testimony of women in baths. And al-Karkhī mentioned that whoever walks in the road wearing only a sirwāl (trousers) with nothing else, his testimony is not accepted because he has abandoned murū’ah (decorum). End.
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof for rejecting testimony due to immodesty. The proof is twofold: 1) Exposing the ‘awrah is ḥarām by textual evidence, and 2) The action of Abū Ḥanīfah narrating a verse (in poetic form) condemning entering the bath without an izār. The commentary connects this to the earlier ruling that women's testimony in baths is rejected. Additionally, al-Karkhī adds that walking in public wearing only trousers (sirwāl) without a loose garment above is a lack of murū’ah (dignity/decency) and voids testimony. The rationale: both actions are either ḥarām (exposing ‘awrah) or contrary to social decorum (tark al-murū’ah), both of which undermine ‘adālah. The authority_weight includes a direct statement from Abū Ḥanīfah (Zahir al-Riwayah) and a later fatwa from al-Karkhī.

Key Terms
مروءة (murū’ah): Decorum and dignity expected of a respectable person; its absence voids ‘adālah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the testimony of a man who walks in the street wearing only trousers (sirwāl) rejected?
A) Because trousers are not permissible for men.
B) Because he has abandoned murū’ah (decorum).
C) Because he is not fully covered.
D) Because he is showing off.

Key Takeaway
Testimony is rejected if the witness displays a lack of modesty in public, either by committing a prohibited act (kashf al-‘awrah) or by abandoning social decorum (murū’ah).

Rejection of Testimony for Ribā and Gambling: The Condition of Notoriety
Ruling with proof on ribā and gambling as grounds for rejecting testimony

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on rejecting testimony for ribā and gambling, and distinguish the condition of notoriety (mashhūr) from a single occurrence.

Core Idea
Testimony is rejected only for those notorious for ribā or gambling, not for an isolated sin.

In this passage, the author Kanz al-Daqā’iq rules that eating ribā or gambling (nard and shatranj) voids a witness's probity. However, the earlier authoritative text al-Aṣl restricts this to those notorious for such acts. Al-Hidāyah supports the restricted view, noting that few people are entirely free from corrupt contracts. Understanding this nuance is crucial for correctly applying the law of testimony.

Guided Path
1
Identify the acts and their rulings
The passage mentions eating ribā and gambling with nard/shatranj as grounds for rejecting testimony. Recognize that both are major sins (kabā'ir) that affect ‘adālah.
Ribā is prohibited by Quran 2:275; gambling is prohibited by Quran 5:90.
2
Locate the primary proof
The author cites Quran 2:275 ('alladhīna ya'kulūn al-ribā') as the proof for prohibition. Understand that the term 'akl' here means 'taking', not literal eating.
The verse is used as a proof text for the gravity of ribā.
3
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
The author (Kanz) makes the ruling absolute: anyone who eats ribā loses probity. However, in al-Aṣl (Zahir al-Riwayah) it is restricted to those notorious (mashhūr) for it. Al-Hidāyah explains that since people rarely escape corrupt contracts, only the habitually sinful are disqualified.
This is a key nuance: a single transaction does not void probity.
4
Identify the tarjīḥ (preferred view)
Al-Hidāyah explicitly prefers the restricted view from al-Aṣl. This becomes the muftā bihi (operative ruling). Understand why: to avoid overly broad disqualification.
The authority weight is Zahir al-Riwayah.
5
Apply to gambling and missing prayer
The passage also mentions gambling and missing prayer as grounds. For gambling, the same logic of notoriety likely applies by extension. The missing prayer condition is specific: one who deliberately delays prayer after hearing adhān loses probity.
These additional grounds are mentioned briefly but follow the same principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (أو يأكل الربا لأنه من الكبائر أي يأخذ القدر الزائد فالمراد بالأكل الأخذ وإنما ذكره تبعاً للآية الذين يأكلون الربا [البقرة : ۲۷٥] وإنما ذكره في الآية لأنه معظم منافع المال ولأن الربا شائع في المطعومات، والمراد بالربا بالقدر الزائد لا الزيادة وهي المرادة في قوله تعالى وحرم الربا [البقرة : ٢٧٥] كما بيناه في بابه. وأطلقه المؤلف تبعاً لكثير، وقيده في الأصل بأن يكون مشهوراً به، وعلله في الهداية بأن الإنسان قل ما ينجو عن مباشرات العقود الفاسدة وكل ذلك ربا ا هـ . وهو أولى مما قيل لأن الربا ليس بحرام محض لأنه يفيد الملك بالقبض كسائر البياعات الفاسدة وإن كان غاصباً مع ذلك فكان ناقصاً في أو يقامر بالنرد والشطرنج أو تفوته الصلاة وليس هو من سمع الأذان فانتظر الإقامة سقطت عدالته ا هـ.
His saying: 'Or he eats ribā because it is among the major sins'—meaning he takes the excess amount. By 'eating' is meant 'taking', and it is mentioned following the verse: 'Those who eat ribā' [2:275]. It is mentioned in the verse because it is the greatest benefit of wealth and because ribā is prevalent in foodstuffs. What is meant by ribā is the excess amount, not the increase that is intended in the verse: 'And Allah has prohibited ribā' [2:275], as we have explained in its chapter. And the author made it absolute following many, but in al-Asl it is restricted by being notorious for it. Al-Hidāyah explained: 'Man rarely escapes engaging in corrupt contracts, and all of that is ribā.' This is more appropriate than what was said, because ribā is not pure ḥarām since it grants ownership by possession like other corrupt sales, even if the person is a usurper along with that, so it is deficient in… Or he gambles with nard and shatranj, or misses the prayer, and he is not among those who heard the adhān and waited for the iqāmah—his probity falls. End.
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding ribā and gambling. The proof for ribā's prohibition is the Quranic verse 2:275. The commentary clarifies that 'eating' ribā means taking the excess (interest), and it is a major sin (kabīrah). However, the author (Ṣāḥib Kanz al-Daqā’iq) made the ruling absolute (the testimony of anyone who eats ribā is rejected), but in al-Aṣl (Zahir al-Riwayah) it is restricted to being notorious (mashhūr bih) for it. Al-Hidāyah explains that since most people engage in some corrupt contracts, only those known for it lose ‘adālah. The text also mentions gambling with nard (backgammon) and shatranj (chess) as a ground for rejection, as these are forms of gambling prohibited by Quran and Sunnah. Missing the prayer (tafūtuhu al-ṣalāh) is also a major sin, and the condition about hearing the adhān and waiting for iqāmah (i.e., deliberately delaying) is mentioned as a specific instance. The authority_weight: the ruling on ribā is from Zahir al-Riwayah (al-Aṣl) with the condition of notoriety; the later text from al-Hidāyah is also mu‘tamad.

Key Terms
ربا (ribā): Usury/interest; any excess in a loan or sale of certain items that is prohibited. Here specifically the taking of an excess amount beyond the principal.
قمار (qimār): Gambling; playing nard (backgammon) or shatranj (chess) for stakes is prohibited and voids ‘adālah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what condition is required for the testimony of someone who eats ribā to be rejected?
A) That he eats it intentionally.
B) That he is notorious (mashhūr) for it.
C) That he rejects its prohibition.
D) That it involves foodstuffs.

Key Takeaway
Testimonial probity is lost only for those notorious for ribā or gambling, not for isolated acts.
