Shahadat — Testimony
intermediate
8 min
Testimony of Ahl al-Ahwa' (People of Desires)
Explaining the Hanafi ruling on accepting testimony from followers of desire

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on accepting testimony from ahl al-ahwa' and apply the distinction between those whose desire leads to kufr and those who do not.

Core Idea
Testimony of ahl al-ahwa' is accepted unless their belief reaches kufr, as proven by the Companions' practice.

This passage clarifies a common misconception: not all followers of desire are disqualified from giving testimony in Hanafi law. The key is whether their desire amounts to disbelief. The proof is drawn from the practice of the Sahaba, who differed and even fought yet their testimony remained valid. The passage also addresses the extreme sect al-Khatabiyyah, whose testimony is rejected due to their known falsehood.

Guided Path
1
Define ahl al-ahwa' and distinguish from fisq
Understand that hawa is blameworthy inclination without Sharia's call, different from open sin (fisq). This sets the scope of the discussion.
The passage defines hawa as a masdar meaning inclination, then specifically blameworthy.
2
State the general Hanafi position
Abu Hanifa and Ibn Abi Layla held that testimony of ahl al-ahwa' is permissible (ja'iz) as per the apparent narration (zahir al-riwayah).
This is the default ruling; exceptions follow.
3
Identify the proof (dalil)
The proof is the practice of the Companions: they differed and fought, yet their testimony against each other was accepted. This is a qiyas showing that even severe ikhtilaf does not invalidate testimony.
The dalil is a historical precedent (thubut qati, dalala zanni).
4
Note the restriction: kufr
If a person's desire leads to kufr (disbelief), the majority (including al-Muhit al-Burhani) rule that his testimony is not accepted. The zahir al-riwayah is interpreted to mean only those not deemed kafir.
This restriction is based on the principle that a kafir's testimony is generally not accepted.
5
Apply to al-Khatabiyyah
Al-Khatabiyyah, an extreme Shi'a sect known for bearing false witness, is an example where testimony is rejected. Their falsehood is established, unlike general ahl al-ahwa'.
The Hanafi view also rejects their riwaya (narrations) because they fabricate hadith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما أوقعه فيه إلا تدينه به وصار كمن يشرب المثلث أو يأكل متروك التسمية عامداً مستبيحاً لذلك بخلاف الفسق من حيث التعاطي والهوى مقصوراً ميل النفس ما تستلذ به من الشهوات من غير داعية الشرع؛ كذا في التقرير. وفي المصباح الهوى مقصوراً مصدر هويته من باب تعب إذا أحببته وعلقت به ثم أطلق على ميل النفس وانحرافها نحو الشيء ثم استعمل في ميل مذموم فيقال اتبع هواه وهو من أهل الأهواء والهواء ممدوداً المسخر بين السماء والأرض والجمع أهوية ا.هـ. أطلقه وقيده في الذخيرة بهوى لا يكفر به صاحبه، وزاد في السراج الوهاج أن لا يكون ماجناً ويكون عدلاً في تعاطيه هو الصحيح ا هـ . القيد في ظاهر الرواية فإن الحاكم الشهيد في الكافي قال وقال أبو حنيفة وابن أبي ليلى : شهادة أصحاب الأهواء جائزة ألا ترى أن أصحاب محمد صلى الله عليه وسلم قد اختلفوا واقتتلوا وشهادة بعضهم على بعض كانت جائزة فليس بين أصحاب الاهواء من الاختلاف أشد مما كان بينهم القتال ا هـ . وفي التقرير أن من وجب إكفاره منهم فالأكثر على عدم قبوله ا هـ. وفي المحيط البرهاني وهو الصحيح وما ذكره في الأصل محمول عليه. وفي النهاية أن أصول الهوى ستة: الجبر والقدر والرفض والخروج والتشبيه والتعطيل ثم كل واحد يصير اثني عشر فرقة ا هـ . وفي الحديث إن بني اسرائيل تفرقت على ثنتين وسبعين فرقة وستفترق أمتي على ثلاث وسبعين فرقة كلها في النار إلا فرقة واحدة قيل من هي يا رسول الله؟ قال : من كان على ما أنا عليه وأصحابي ا هـ . والخطابية قوم من الروافض ينسبون إلى أبي الخطاب يدينون بشهادة الزور لمن وافقهم على مخالفيهم، وقيل يشهدون لمن حلف لهم أنه محق ويقولون المسلم لا يحلف كاذباً فتمكنت شبهة الكذب فيها. وفي العتابية: هم قوم من الروافض يكفرون بالصغائر. وفي الينابيع أن الخطابية انقرضوا وفنوا للآية الشريفة ولن يجعل الله للكافرين على المؤمنين سبيلا ﴾ [النساء : ١٤١] وفي التقرير : ويلحق صاحب بهم الإلهام فلا تقبل شهادته وأما روايته فالمختار في المذهب عدم قبولها لأنهم يحتاجون إلى المحاجة فيحتاجون إلى التقول والكذب على رسول الله الله بخلاف الشهادة اهـ. والمنقول عند الشافعية عدم قبول شهادة الخطابية إلا من صرح منهم بالمشاهدة ولم أره لأصحابنا
And nothing caused him to fall into it except his adherence to it, and he became like one who drinks thuluth (a wine) or eats meat on which the name of Allah was deliberately not recited, considering it permissible. This is contrary to fisq (open sin) in terms of engagement and desire, which is limited to the soul's inclination towards what it finds pleasurable from desires without the call of the Sharia; thus in al-Taqrir. And in al-Misbah: al-hawa (desire) is a limited source (masdar) from 'hawitu' (I loved) from the paradigm of ta'iba (to tire) when you love something and are attached to it. Then it was used for the inclination of the soul and its deviation towards something. Then it was used for a blameworthy inclination, so it is said 'he followed his desire' and he is from ahl al-ahwa'. And al-hawa' (with madd) is the air subjugated between heaven and earth, plural 'ahwiyah'. End. It was left unrestricted and then restricted in al-Dhakhira to a desire that does not cause its holder to become a disbeliever, and he added in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj that he should not be a joker and should be just in his engagement; that is correct. End. The restriction is in the apparent narration, for al-Hakim al-Shahid in al-Kafi said: Abu Hanifa and Ibn Abi Layla said: Testimony of ahl al-ahwa' (people of desires) is permissible. Do you not see that the Companions of Muhammad (peace be upon him) differed and fought, and their testimony against each other was permissible. There is nothing among ahl al-ahwa' of difference more severe than what was between them of fighting. End. And in al-Taqrir: Among them, the one whose disbelief is established, the majority is on the non-acceptance of his testimony. End. And in al-Muhit al-Burhani: It is the correct view, and what was mentioned in al-Asl is interpreted accordingly. And in al-Nihaya: The roots of desire are six: jabr (predestination), qadar (free will), rafd (rejection), khuruj (outcry), tashbih (anthropomorphism), and ta'til (negation). Then each becomes twelve sects. End. And in the hadith: 'The Children of Israel divided into seventy-two sects, and my Ummah will divide into seventy-three sects, all in the Fire except one.' It was said: 'Who are they, O Messenger of Allah?' He said: 'Those who are upon what I and my Companions are upon.' End. And al-Khatabiyyah are a people from al-Rawafid (a Shi'a sect) attributed to Abu al-Khattab. They believe in bearing false witness for those who agree with them against their opponents. And it is said they testify for one who swears to them that he is right, and they say a Muslim does not swear falsely. So the doubtful charge of lying is established against them. And in al-Atabiyyah: They are a people from al-Rawafid who deem others disbelievers for minor sins. And in al-Yanabi': That the Khatabiyyah have ceased and perished due to the noble verse 'And never will Allah give the disbelievers over the believers a way' [An-Nisa 4:141]. And in al-Taqrir: And ilham is annexed to them? So his testimony is not accepted. As for his narration, the chosen view in the madhhab is its non-acceptance because they need to debate, so they need to fabricate and lie about the Messenger of Allah, unlike testimony. End. And it is transmitted from the Shafi'is that the testimony of al-Khatabiyyah is not accepted except from those among them who explicitly state that they witnessed, and I have not seen that from our companions.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof on the acceptance of testimony from ahl al-ahwa' (people of desires). The author begins by defining hawa as blameworthy inclination of the soul, distinct from open fisq. The Hanafi position, attributed to Abu Hanifa and Ibn Abi Layla, is that their testimony is generally permissible. The proof is the practice of the Sahaba who differed and fought yet their testimony against each other remained valid. However, a restriction is made: if a person's desire leads to disbelief (kufr), the majority hold that his testimony is not accepted. The correct view in al-Muhit al-Burhani is that the ruling in the original text (al-Asl) is interpreted to mean that. The passage then lists six roots of desire mentioned in al-Nihaya. It cites the hadith of 73 sects to highlight that the saved sect is the one following the Prophet and his Companions. Then it discusses al-Khatabiyyah, a sect of rawafid who practice false witness for their allies. Their testimony is rejected because they lie. The Hanafi position is that their narration (riwaya) is also not accepted because they are prone to fabricating hadith. The Shafi'i view is mentioned but not adopted by Hanafis. Overall, the lesson emphasizes that not all ahl al-ahwa' are rejected; only those whose beliefs lead to kufr or who are known for falsehood.

Key Terms
الهوى (al-hawa): Blameworthy inclination of the soul towards desires without the call of Sharia; a source of innovation.
أهل الأهواء (ahl al-ahwa'): People who follow their desires in religious matters, often innovators or members of sects.
الخطابية (al-Khatabiyyah): A sect of rawafid (extreme Shi'a) who bear false witness for their allies.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school as presented in this passage, what is the ruling on accepting testimony from a person who follows his desires (sahib hawa) but is not considered a disbeliever?
A) It is rejected unconditionally.
B) It is permissible, and the precedent of the Companions supports this.
C) It is valid only if he is a mujtahid.
D) It is accepted only if he repents.

Key Takeaway
Testimony of ahl al-ahwa' is generally accepted unless their desire constitutes kufr or they are from a group known for falsehood.

Shahadat — Testimony
beginner

General Ruling: Dhimmi Testimony Against His Own Kind
Ruling with proof on dhimmi testimony against his own kind

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on dhimmi testimony against another dhimmi and identify the proofs and conditions that support it.

Core Idea
A dhimmi's testimony against his own kind is accepted due to guardianship and universal prohibition of lying.

In this lesson, we examine the foundational Hanafi ruling on the testimony of a dhimmi (non-Muslim under Islamic protection) against another dhimmi. The passage presents the key proofs from the Prophet's practice and the concept of wilayah, while clarifying the exclusion of apostates and the limitation that such testimony is not accepted against Muslims. This is a classic ruling-with-proof structure that demonstrates how Hanafi jurists derive procedural law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine that the act is the testimony of a dhimmi against another dhimmi. The ruling: it is permissible to accept such testimony. This is a ruling of acceptance, not an obligation.
The text begins with 'والذمي على مثله' (the dhimmi against his like).
2
Locate the primary dalil
Find the proof cited: the Prophet (pbuh) allowed Christians to testify against each other. This is a hadith reference (though not quoted verbatim).
The passage says 'لأنه عليه الصلاة والسلام أجاز شهادة النصارى بعضهم على بعض'.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Explain how the dalil proves the ruling: the Prophet's permission establishes permissibility. Additionally, the dhimmi has wilayah (legal authority) over himself and his minor children, qualifying him to testify. Fisq due to disbelief is not a bar because he avoids what his religion forbids, and lying is forbidden in all religions.
The argument uses both direct prophetic action and general principles.
4
Note the restrictions
Identify what the ruling is restricted to: (1) only against a dhimmi, not a Muslim; (2) only for a dhimmi, not an apostate (murtadd). The apostate has no testimony at all, even against his own kind, according to the correct view.
The text explicitly restricts to dhimmi and to 'against his like'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (والذمي على مثله) لأنه عليه الصلاة والسلام أجاز شهادة النصارى بعضهم على بعض ولأنه من أهل الولاية على نفسه وأولاده الصغار فيكون من أهل الشهادة على جنسه والفسق من حيث الاعتقاد غير مانع لأنه يجتنب عما يعتقده محرم دينه والكذب محظور الأديان. قيد بالذمي لأن المرتد لا شهادة له لأنه لا ولاية له واختلفوا في شهادة مرتد على مثله والأصح عدم قبولها بحال؛ كذا في المحيط البرهاني. وقيد بقوله على مثله لأنها لا تقبل على مسلم للآية ولن يجعل الله للكافرين على المؤمنين سبيلا ولأنه لا ولاية له بالإضافة إليه ولأنه يتقول عليه لأنه يغيظه قهره إياه. وفي الولوالجية : نصرانيان شهدا على نصراني بقطع يد أو قصاص ثم أسلم المشهود عليه بعد القضاء بطلت الشهادة لأن الإمضاء من القضاء في العقوبات ا هـ . وفي تلخيص الجامع للصدر سليمان نصراني مات عن مائة فأقام مسلم شاهدين عليه بمائة ومسلم ونصراني بمثله فالثلثان له والباقي بينهما والشركة لا تمنع لأنها بإقراره بخلاف الإقرار لوارثه وأجنبي نظيره أقر لأجنبي في مرضه فأقر لوراثه، وعن أبي يوسف النصف لهما للاستواء، لإظهار الفسق بالفعل قول المصنف : (والذمي على مثله قال الرملي وفي التتارخانية شهادة أهل الذمة بعضهم على بعض مقبولة. وفي التجريد : إذا كانوا عدولاً في دينهم اتفقت مللهم أو اختلفت. وفي التفريد : وعند مالك تقبل إذا اتفقت مللهم وعند الشافعي لا تقبل أصلاً اهـ. وكتب الرملي أيضاً : وإن اختلفا ملة كاليهود مع النصارى ؛ كذا في شرح تنوير الأبصار، ومثله في لسان الحكام لابن الشحنه وشرحي المجمع للمصنف وابن ملك وكثير من الكتب كالغياثية والكفاية ودارا كما في العناية والكفاية وكثير من الكتب .اهـ. قلت والظاهر أن العداوة بين اليهود والنصارى دينية وإلا لم تقبل فتأمل. قوله : (لأنه يغيظه قهره إياه قال الرملي : الضمير في «أنه» و «يغيظه راجع للذمي وفي «قهره» راجع للمسلم أي لأنه بسبب قهر المسلم إياه وإذلاله له يتقول عليه بخلاف ملل الكفر لأن ملة الإسلام قاهرة للكل فلم يبق لهم غيرة يستظهرون بها قوله : فالثلثان له والباقي بينهما) أي الثلثان للمسلم المنفرد والباقي للمسلم والنصراني؛ ذكر في الذخيرة عبارة الجامع ولم يبين وجه ذلك، ثم ذكر مسألة أخرى وهي : نصراني مات وترك ألف درهم وأقام مسلم شهوداً من النصارى على ألف على الميت ونصراني آخرين كذلك يدفع الألف المتروكة للمسلم ولا يتحاصان فيها عنده، وعند أبي يوسف، يتحاصان، والخلاف راجع إلى أن بينة النصراني مقبولة عنده في حق إثبات الدين على الميت لا في حق إثبات الشركة بينه وبين المسلم وعلى قول الثاني مقبولة فيهما اهـ . لكن يبقى وجه اختصاص
His statement: '(And the dhimmi against his like) because the Prophet (peace be upon him) permitted the testimony of Christians against each other, and because he is among the people of guardianship over himself and his minor children, so he is among the people of testimony against his own kind. And fisq (sin) due to belief is not a preventer because he avoids what he believes is prohibited in his religion, and lying is forbidden in all religions. It is restricted to dhimmi because the apostate has no testimony as he has no guardianship. And they differed regarding the testimony of an apostate against his like, and the most correct is its absolute non-acceptance; thus in al-Muhit al-Burhani. And it is restricted by his statement 'against his like' because it is not accepted against a Muslim due to the verse 'And never will Allah give the disbelievers over the believers a way' (4:141), and because he has no guardianship relative to him, and because he would fabricate against him because his (the Muslim's) domination angers him. And in al-Walwaljiyyah: Two Christians testified against a Christian for amputation of a hand or retaliation, then the accused (against whom the verdict was issued) accepted Islam after the judgment; the testimony becomes void because execution of judgment in punishments (is not carried out after conversion). End. And in Talkhis al-Jami' by al-Sadr Sulayman: A Christian died leaving 100 (dirhams). A Muslim brought two witnesses claiming 100 against the deceased, and another (Muslim and a Christian) brought a similar claim; then two-thirds go to him (the first Muslim) and the rest between them (the second Muslim and the Christian). And partnership (in debt) is not a bar because it is by his own admission, unlike admission to his heir and a stranger; analogous to one who admitted to a stranger in his terminal illness then admitted to his heirs. And from Abu Yusuf: half to both (the two claimants collectively) due to equality. To show fisq by action? The author's statement: '(And the dhimmi against his like)' said al-Ramli: And in al-TatarKhaniyya: Testimony of ahl al-dhimma against each other is accepted. In al-Tajrid: If they are just in their religion, whether their sects agree or differ. In al-Tafrid: According to Malik, it is accepted if their sects agree; according to al-Shafi'i, it is not accepted at all. End. And al-Ramli also wrote: Even if their sects differ, like Jews with Christians; thus in Sharh Tanwir al-Absar, and similar in Lisan al-Hukkam by Ibn al-Shihnah, and in the two Sharhs of al-Majma' by the author and Ibn Malik, and many books like al-Ghiyathiyya, al-Kifaya, and Dara; as in al-Inaya and al-Kifaya and many books. End. I say: It is apparent that the enmity between Jews and Christians is religious, otherwise it would not be accepted; so reflect. His statement: '(Because his domination angers him)' said al-Ramli: The pronoun in 'annahu' and 'yughizuhu' refers to the dhimmi, and in 'qahruhu' refers to the Muslim, i.e., because the Muslim's domination and humiliation of him causes him to fabricate against him, unlike religions of disbelief because the religion of Islam overwhelms all, so they have no religious zeal to use. His statement: 'So two-thirds to him and the rest between them' meaning two-thirds to the lone Muslim and the rest to the Muslim and the Christian. In al-Dhakhira, the wording of al-Jami' is mentioned without clarifying its reasoning. Then he mentioned another issue: A Christian died leaving 1000 dirhams. A Muslim brought witnesses from Christians for 1000 against the deceased, and another (two Christians) similarly. The 1000 left is given to the Muslim (the first) and they do not share according to him (Abu Hanifa). According to Abu Yusuf, they share. The dispute reverts to whether the evidence of the Christian is accepted according to him (Abu Hanifa) in terms of establishing debt against the deceased but not in terms of establishing partnership between him and the Muslim; according to the second (Abu Yusuf), it is accepted in both. End. But the reasoning for exclusivity remains.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof on the testimony of a dhimmi (non-Muslim subject) against his own kind. The author explains that the Prophet (peace be upon him) allowed Christians to testify against each other, and since a dhimmi has legal guardianship over himself and his minor children, he is qualified to testify against his own kind. The sin of disbelief does not bar testimony because he avoids what his religion forbids, and lying is forbidden in all religions. The rule is restricted to dhimmis; apostates have no testimony. There is a dispute about whether an apostate can testify against another apostate; the correct view is absolute rejection. Testimony of a dhimmi is not accepted against a Muslim, based on the Quranic verse (4:141) and because the dhimmi lacks guardianship over a Muslim and may fabricate out of anger. The passage then discusses a case from al-Walwalijiyya: if two Christians testify against a Christian for amputation or retaliation, and the accused converts to Islam after the judgment, the testimony is void because the punishment cannot be carried out after conversion. Another case from Talkhis al-Jami': a Christian dies leaving 100 dirhams. A Muslim claims 100, and another claimant (a Muslim and a Christian) also claims 100. The ruling is two-thirds to the lone Muslim and one-third to the second claimant group; but Abu Yusuf says half to each claimant. The author explains the reasoning based on partnership by admission. The passage then presents the inter-madhhab dispute: Hanafis accept testimony of dhimmis against each other regardless of whether their sects are the same (Jew vs Christian) or different, as long as they are just in their religion. Malik accepts only if sects agree; Shafi'i rejects absolutely. The Hanafi position is favored. Finally, the text discusses the reason for rejecting dhimmi testimony against Muslims: the dhimmi's anger at Muslim domination leads to potential lying. The last case involves a debt claim by a Muslim with Christian witnesses against a deceased Christian, and another claim by two Christians. Abu Hanifa gives the entire estate to the Muslim, while Abu Yusuf gives half each. The dispute centers on whether Christian testimony can establish partnership for the Christian claimant. The passage ends abruptly before completing the reasoning.

Key Terms
الذمي (al-dhimmi): A non-Muslim living under the protection of an Islamic state who pays jizya.
المرتد (al-murtadd): An apostate, one who leaves Islam.
الولاية (al-wilayah): Legal authority or capability; used here as the basis for eligibility to testify.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling in this passage, why is the testimony of a dhimmi against another dhimmi accepted?
A) Because all dhimmis are considered just in Islam.
B) Because the Prophet explicitly testified for dhimmis.
C) Because the dhimmi has wilayah over himself and his children, and lying is forbidden in all religions.
D) Because dhimmis are always truthful in financial matters.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school accepts testimony of a dhimmi against his own kind based on Prophetic precedent and the principle of wilayah, rejecting common objections about disbelief and sect differences.

Shahadat — Testimony

Restrictions on Dhimmi Testimony: Against Muslims and After Conversion
Ruling with proof on restrictions of dhimmi testimony

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why dhimmi testimony is not accepted against Muslims and what happens if the accused converts to Islam after judgment in a hadd case.

Core Idea
Dhimmi testimony against a Muslim is rejected to prevent bias and uphold Quranic principle; conversion after judgment voids testimony in hudud.

This lesson delves into two important restrictions on dhimmi testimony highlighted in the passage: the absolute bar against testifying against a Muslim, and the effect of the accused's conversion to Islam after a judgment in a hadd case. The Quranic verse 4:141 provides the foundational proof, while practical reasoning about potential bias supports the rule. The sub-case from al-Walwalijiyyah illustrates a specific application.

Guided Path
1
State the restriction: dhimmi vs Muslim
Identify that the passage explicitly rules that dhimmi testimony is not accepted against a Muslim. This is a specific exclusion from the general acceptance of dhimmi testimony.
The text says: 'لأنها لا تقبل على مسلم' (it is not accepted against a Muslim).
2
Identify the primary dalil
Find the Quranic verse cited: 'ولن يجعل الله للكافرين على المؤمنين سبيلا' (And never will Allah give the disbelievers over the believers a way) – Quran 4:141.
This verse is a key textual proof (dalil naqli).
3
Explain the rational reasoning (waliyyah and bias)
Two reasons are given: (a) the dhimmi has no guardianship (wilayah) over a Muslim, so he lacks qualification to testify against him; (b) the dhimmi may fabricate testimony because the Muslim's dominance (qahr) angers him, leading to bias.
This shows the Hanafi method of combining textual and rational proofs.
4
Apply the sub-case: conversion after judgment
Consider the scenario from al-Walwalijiyyah: two Christians testify against a Christian for amputation or retaliation, and the accused converts to Islam after the judgment. The testimony becomes void. Reason: qisas (retaliation) cannot be carried out after conversion because the hadd punishment is no longer applicable.
This demonstrates how a change in the status of the accused affects the validity of the testimony.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

لا تقبل على مسلم للآية ولن يجعل الله للكافرين على المؤمنين سبيلا ولأنه لا ولاية له بالإضافة إليه ولأنه يتقول عليه لأنه يغيظه قهره إياه. وفي الولوالجية : نصرانيان شهدا على نصراني بقطع يد أو قصاص ثم والكفاية وكثير من الكتب .اهـ. قلت والظاهر أن العداوة بين اليهود والنصارى دينية وإلا لم تقبل فتأمل. قوله : (لأنه يغيظه قهره إياه قال الرملي : الضمير في «أنه» و «يغيظه راجع للذمي وفي «قهره» راجع للمسلم أي لأنه بسبب قهر المسلم إياه وإذلاله له يتقول عليه بخلاف ملل الكفر لأن ملة الإسلام قاهرة للكل فلم يبق لهم غيرة يستظهرون بها قوله : فالثلثان له والباقي بينهما) أي الثلثان للمسلم المنفرد والباقي للمسلم And from Abu Yusuf: half to both (the two claimants collectively) due to equality. To show fisq by action? The author's statement: '(And the dhimmi against his like)' said al-Ramli: And in al-TatarKhaniyya: Testimony of ahl al-dhimma against each other is accepted. In al-Tajrid: If they are just in their religion, whether their sects agree or differ. In al-Tafrid: According to Malik, it is accepted if their sects agree; according to al-Shafi'i, it is not accepted at all. End. And al-Ramli also wrote: Even if their sects differ, like Jews with Christians; thus in Sharh Tanwir al-Absar, and similar in Lisan al-Hukkam by Ibn al-Shihnah, and in the two Sharhs of al-Majma' by the author and Ibn Malik, and many books like al-Ghiyathiyya, al-Kifaya, and Dara; as in al-Inaya and al-Kifaya and many books. End. I say: It is apparent that the enmity between Jews and Christians is religious, otherwise it would not be accepted; so reflect. His statement: '(Because his domination angers him)' said al-Ramli: The pronoun in 'annahu' and 'yughizuhu' refers to the dhimmi, and in 'qahruhu' refers to the Muslim, i.e., because the Muslim's domination and humiliation of him causes him to fabricate against him, unlike religions of disbelief because the religion of Islam overwhelms all, so they have no religious zeal to use. His statement: 'So two-thirds to him and the rest between them' meaning two-thirds to the lone Muslim and the rest to the Muslim and the Christian. In al-Dhakhira, the wording of al-Jami' is mentioned without clarifying its reasoning. Then he mentioned another issue: A Christian died leaving 1000 dirhams. A Muslim brought witnesses from Christians for 1000 against the deceased, and another (two Christians) similarly. The 1000 left is given to the Muslim (the first) and they do not share according to him (Abu Hanifa). According to Abu Yusuf, they share. The dispute reverts to whether the evidence of the Christian is accepted according to him (Abu Hanifa) in terms of establishing debt against the deceased but not in terms of establishing partnership between him and the Muslim; according to the second (Abu Yusuf), it is accepted in both. End. But the reasoning for exclusivity remains.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof on the testimony of a dhimmi (non-Muslim subject) against his own kind. The author explains that the Prophet (peace be upon him) allowed Christians to testify against each other, and since a dhimmi has legal guardianship over himself and his minor children, he is qualified to testify against his own kind. The sin of disbelief does not bar testimony because he avoids what his religion forbids, and lying is forbidden in all religions. The rule is restricted to dhimmis; apostates have no testimony. There is a dispute about whether an apostate can testify against another apostate; the correct view is absolute rejection. Testimony of a dhimmi is not accepted against a Muslim, based on the Quranic verse (4:141) and because the dhimmi lacks guardianship over a Muslim and may fabricate out of anger. The passage then discusses a case from al-Walwalijiyya: if two Christians testify against a Christian for amputation or retaliation, and the accused converts to Islam after the judgment, the testimony is void because the punishment cannot be carried out after conversion. Another case from Talkhis al-Jami': a Christian dies leaving 100 dirhams. A Muslim claims 100, and another claimant (a Muslim and a Christian) also claims 100. The ruling is two-thirds to the lone Muslim and one-third to the second claimant group; but Abu Yusuf says half to each claimant. The author explains the reasoning based on partnership by admission. The passage then presents the inter-madhhab dispute: Hanafis accept testimony of dhimmis against each other regardless of whether their sects are the same (Jew vs Christian) or different, as long as they are just in their religion. Malik accepts only if sects agree; Shafi'i rejects absolutely. The Hanafi position is favored. Finally, the text discusses the reason for rejecting dhimmi testimony against Muslims: the dhimmi's anger at Muslim domination leads to potential lying. The last case involves a debt claim by a Muslim with Christian witnesses against a deceased Christian, and another claim by two Christians. Abu Hanifa gives the entire estate to the Muslim, while Abu Yusuf gives half each. The dispute centers on whether Christian testimony can establish partnership for the Christian claimant. The passage ends abruptly before completing the reasoning.

Key Terms
الذمي (al-dhimmi): A non-Muslim living under the protection of an Islamic state who pays jizya.
المرتد (al-murtadd): An apostate, one who leaves Islam.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is dhimmi testimony not accepted against a Muslim? (Choose the best answer.)
A) Because dhimmis are always liars.
B) Because the Quran prohibits giving disbelievers any way over believers, and because the dhimmi lacks guardianship and may be biased.
C) Because the Prophet forbade it in a specific hadith.
D) Because Muslims are inherently superior and cannot be judged by dhimmis.

Key Takeaway
Dhimmi testimony is strictly limited to cases against other dhimmis; it is rejected against Muslims due to Quranic injunction, lack of guardianship, and risk of bias, and conversion of the accused after judgment voids testimony in hudud.

Shahadat — Testimony

Inter-Madhhab Dispute: Dhimmi Testimony Across Schools
Inter-madhhab dispute on dhimmi testimony

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to compare the Hanafi, Maliki, and Shafi'i positions on dhimmi testimony and analyze the Hanafi tarjih.

Core Idea
Hanafis accept dhimmi testimony broadly; Shafi'is reject it; Malikis condition it on same sect.

This lesson examines the inter-madhhab dispute on whether the testimony of a non-Muslim (dhimmi) is acceptable. The passage explicitly contrasts the Hanafi position (accepted regardless of sect) with the Maliki (accepted only if sects agree) and Shafi'i (never accepted). We will explore the proofs behind each view and the Hanafi response that leads to tarjih.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
The Hanafi school holds that the testimony of dhimmis against each other is accepted, whether their sects agree or differ, as long as they are just in their own religion.
Quoted from al-Tajrid: 'إذا كانوا عدولاً في دينهم اتفقت مللهم أو اختلفت'.
2
Identify the opposing schools and their views
Malik: accepted only if the sects agree (e.g., Jew vs Jew, Christian vs Christian). Shafi'i: not accepted at all.
From al-Tafrid: 'عند مالك تقبل إذا اتفقت مللهم وعند الشافعي لا تقبل أصلاً'.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response to each
Hanafis argue that enmity between sects (e.g., Jew vs Christian) is religious, but it does not prevent testimony because their own religion forbids lying. The author (al-Ramli) comments that the apparent enmity is religious, and even if it were personal, the rule holds because the basis is wilayah, not friendship.
The passage says 'والظاهر أن العداوة بين اليهود والنصارى دينية وإلا لم تقبل فتأمل' – hinting that even if enmity were personal, the ruling would remain.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference)
In practice, a Hanafi court will accept a Jew's testimony against a Christian; a Maliki court will reject it unless both are same sect; a Shafi'i court will reject all dhimmi testimony, often forcing reliance on Muslim witnesses or other evidence.
This affects litigation in mixed-faith communities.

His statement: '(And the dhimmi against his like) because the Prophet (peace be upon him) permitted the testimony of Christians against each other, and because he is among the people of guardianship over himself and his minor children, so he is among the people of testimony against his own kind. Testimony of a Dhimmi Against Another Dhimmi
Explanation of the ruling with proofs and conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the testimony of a dhimmi against another dhimmi and apply the conditions that limit its acceptance.

Core Idea
A dhimmi’s testimony is valid against another dhimmi, but not against a Muslim.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains a key rule in the law of evidence: when a non-Muslim protected subject (dhimmi) can serve as a witness. The author provides two proofs (hadith and analogy), then imposes crucial restrictions: testimony is only against another dhimmi, not a Muslim, and requires the witness to be dhimmi, not an apostate. Understanding these limitations is essential for applying Hanafi law in courts involving non-Muslim litigants.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what act is being ruled upon. Here, the act is a dhimmi testifying against another dhimmi. The ruling is that such testimony is accepted (sahih) in court.
The passage states this explicitly at the beginning.
2
Locate the dalil
Find the evidence cited. The author provides two proofs: a hadith that the Prophet (peace be upon him) accepted testimony of Christians against each other, and an analogy (qiyas) based on legal authority (wilayah) over oneself and one's children.
The hadith is transmitted as an action of the Prophet, so it is a Sunnah ahad.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Explain how each proof leads to the ruling. The hadith shows permissibility through prophetic practice. The analogy argues that if a dhimmi has authority over himself and his children, he is fit to testify against his own kind, as testimony requires a degree of legal capacity.
The analogy is based on the illah of having wilayah over oneself and dependents.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Identify the points of disagreement. The Hanafi school accepts testimony even if the sects differ. The Maliki school restricts it to same sect, and the Shafi'i school rejects it entirely. Also, the passage notes that a dhimmi cannot testify against a Muslim.
The disagreement is based on different interpretations of the Quranic verse and the scope of analogy.

Book of Testimonies, chapter on whose testimony is accepted and whose not. And the dhimmi [is accepted] against his like, and the harbi against his like, and one who commits a minor sin if he avoids major sins, and the uncircumcised, the argumentation? They need falsehood and lying against the Messenger of God, peace be upon him, unlike testimony. End. And what is transmitted from the Shafi'is is non-acceptance of testimony of the Khattabiyyah except those who explicitly state witnessing, and I have not seen it for our companions. His statement: (And the dhimmi against his like) because the Prophet, peace be upon him, permitted the testimony of Christians against each other, and because he has authority over himself and his young children, so he is fit to testify against his kind, and fisq in terms of belief is not a preventer because he avoids what he believes is forbidden in his religion, and lying is prohibited by all religions. He restricted with 'dhimmi' because the apostate has no testimony because he has no authority, and they differed on testimony of an apostate against his like, and the soundest is non-acceptance in any case; as in al-Muhit al-Burhani. And he restricted with 'against his like' because it is not accepted against a Muslim due to the verse 'And never will Allah give the disbelievers over the believers a way' [an-Nisa 141], and because he has no authority relative to him, and because he would lie against him because his subjugation angers him. And in al-Walwalijiyya: Two Christians testified against a Christian for amputation of hand or qisas, then the accused converted to Islam after the judgment; the testimony is invalidated because execution is part of judgment in penalties. End. And in Talkhis al-Jami' by al-Sadr Sulayman: A Christian died leaving one hundred [dirhams], and a Muslim brought two witnesses against him for one hundred, and a Muslim and a Christian brought similar [witnesses]; then two-thirds are for him and the remainder between them, and partnership does not prevent because it is by his acknowledgement, unlike acknowledgement for his heir and a stranger; its parallel is acknowledgement to a stranger in his illness then acknowledgement to his heirs. And from Abu Yusuf: half for them due to equality. To show fisq by action, the statement of the author: (And the dhimmi against his like). Al-Ramli said: And in al-Tatarkhaniyya, testimony of ahl al-dhimma against each other is accepted. In al-Tajrid: if they are just in their religion, whether their sects agree or differ. In al-Tafrid: and according to Malik, it is accepted if their sects agree, and according to al-Shafi'i, it is not accepted at all. End. And al-Ramli also wrote: even if they differ in sect, like Jews with Christians; as in Sharh Tanwir al-Absar, and similar in Lisan al-Hukkam by Ibn al-Shihna, and the two commentaries of al-Majma' by the author and Ibn Malik, and many books like al-Ghiyathiyya and al-Kifaya and Dara[?] as in al-Inaya and al-Kifaya and many books. End. I say: and it is apparent that the enmity between Jews and Christians is religious, otherwise it would not be accepted, so consider. His statement: (because his subjugation angers him) al-Ramli said: the pronoun in 'he' and 'angers him' refers to the dhimmi, and in 'his subjugation' refers to the Muslim, i.e., because due to the Muslim's subjugation and humiliation of him, he would lie against him, unlike the sects of disbelief because the religion of Islam dominates all, so they have no zeal to use to prevail.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq is a commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, dealing with the chapter on testimony. The author begins by stating the ruling: a dhimmi's testimony is accepted against another dhimmi. He provides two proofs: first, a hadith that the Prophet (peace be upon him) accepted the testimony of Christians against each other; second, an analogy: since a dhimmi has legal authority over himself and his minor children, he is fit to testify against his own kind. The author then notes that fisq in belief (kufr) does not prevent acceptance because dhimmis avoid what they believe is sinful, and lying is universally prohibited. He adds restrictions: the witness must be a dhimmi, not an apostate, because an apostate has no legal authority; the testimony is only against another dhimmi, not against a Muslim, due to the Quranic verse 'Allah will never give the disbelievers a way over the believers' and because the dhimmi would be tempted to lie out of anger due to Muslim dominance. He then cites a case from al-Walwalijiyya: if two Christians testify against a Christian for amputation or qisas, and the accused converts to Islam after judgment, the testimony is void because execution is part of the penalty. The passage then includes a long case from Talkhis al-Jami' about a deceased Christian with multiple creditors, which illustrates the application of these rules. The author also discusses the intra-madhhab dispute between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf on the division of the estate. The inter-madhhab dispute is then presented: the Hanafi school accepts dhimmi testimony against another dhimmi regardless of difference in sect; Malik accepts only if they share the same sect; Shafi'i rejects it entirely. Al-Ramli adds that the enmity between Jews and Christians is religious, so if they were not enemies, their testimony might not be accepted. Finally, the author explains the phrase about anger: the dhimmi's anger at being subjugated by the Muslim leads him to lie, so his testimony is not accepted against a Muslim. However, among different disbelieving sects, this motive is absent because Islam dominates them all, so their testimony against each other is accepted.

Key Terms
الذمي (al-dhimmi): A non-Muslim subject living under Muslim rule with protection (dhimma).
المرتد (al-murtadd): An apostate from Islam.
الولاية (al-wilayah): Legal authority or capacity to act on one's own or others' behalf.
الشهادة (al-shahadah): Testimony in court.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is a dhimmi's testimony accepted against another dhimmi but not against a Muslim?
A) Because dhimmis have no authority over Muslims, and they might lie out of anger due to Muslim dominance.
B) Because the Quran explicitly forbids accepting dhimmi testimony against Muslims.
C) Because dhimmis are always considered unjust in the Hanafi school.
D) Because the Prophet (peace be upon him) only allowed testimony of dhimmis against each other, not against Muslims.

Key Takeaway
A dhimmi's testimony is valid against another dhimmi, but not against a Muslim, due to lack of wilayah and risk of falsehood.

Dhimmi Testimony: Proving Debt vs. Proving Partnership
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the scope of dhimmi testimony in debt claims.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf on the scope of dhimmi testimony and apply them to a case of competing Muslim and dhimmi creditors.

Core Idea
Dhimmi testimony can prove a debt against a dhimmi but cannot prove partnership between a dhimmi and a Muslim according to Abu Hanifa.

In this lesson, we explore a key intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the evidentiary value of dhimmi testimony. The passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq tackles a complex case where a Muslim and a dhimmi both claim debt from a deceased dhimmi, each presenting dhimmi witnesses. The dispute centers on whether dhimmi testimony can establish not only the debt but also a partnership between the dhimmi and the Muslim. Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf differ, leading to different distribution outcomes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the case and the claimants
The case involves a deceased dhimmi leaving assets, with two sets of claimants: a single Muslim claimant with two dhimmi witnesses, and a pair of claimants (one Muslim and one dhimmi) also with two dhimmi witnesses.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position
Abu Hanifa holds that dhimmi testimony proves the debt against the deceased but does not prove that the dhimmi claimant is a partner with the Muslim claimant. Therefore, the single Muslim's claim is proven fully, and he takes the entire estate; the dhimmi's claim fails for lack of evidence of partnership.
The key distinction: acceptance for debt, but not for partnership.
3
State Abu Yusuf's position
Abu Yusuf accepts dhimmi testimony for both proving the debt and establishing partnership, so both claimants share proportionally (tahassus).
This leads to a different distribution: the Muslim and dhimmi share the estate equally.
4
Understand the reasoning (ta'lil)
The underlying principle is that dhimmi testimony is proof for a Muslim (if it benefits him) but not against him. Therefore, it cannot be used to prove that a dhimmi shares a claim with a Muslim, because that would potentially harm the Muslim by reducing his share.
This reasoning is analogized from the rules of debt in health and illness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : فالثلثان له والباقي بينهما) أي الثلثان للمسلم المنفرد والباقي للمسلم والنصراني؛ ذكر في الذخيرة عبارة الجامع ولم يبين وجه ذلك، ثم ذكر مسألة أخرى وهي : نصراني مات وترك ألف درهم وأقام مسلم شهوداً من النصارى على ألف على الميت ونصراني آخرين كذلك يدفع الألف المتروكة للمسلم ولا يتحاصان فيها عنده، وعند أبي يوسف، يتحاصان، والخلاف راجع إلى أن بينة النصراني مقبولة عنده في حق إثبات الدين على الميت لا في حق إثبات الشركة بينه وبين المسلم وعلى قول الثاني مقبولة فيهما اهـ . لكن يبقى وجه اختصاص المسلم المنفرد بالثلثين في مسألتنا ولعله هو أن البينة تقتضي أن لكل من الثلاثة المدعين ثلث المائة لكن الشهادة الثانية لا تثبت مشاركة النصراني لكل من المسلمين فيعود الثلث الذي كان يستحقه للمسلم المنفرد، وإنما لا يعود منه للمسلم الآخر شيء لأنه مقر بأن له حقاً في المال بقدر حقه ولهذا النصراني ويقاسمه في الثلث الذي أخذه لإقراره بأنه شريكه فيما له على الميت فلم تكن يرجع مشاركته له بالبينة تأمل. ثم رأيت الرملي قال عبارة التلخيص : كافر مات عن مائة فأقام مسلم كافرين بمائة وأقام مسلم وكافر كذلك فثلثاها للمنفرد والثلث للشريكين عكس ما لو كان المنفرد كافراً وشهودا الشريكين مسلمان لأن شهادة الكافر حجة للمسلم لا عليه فضرب كل مسلم فيها بقدر حقه أولاً وكل كافر في الباقي كما في دين الصحة والمرض وقاسم الشريك شريكه لكن بحجة الزعم دون الشهادة اهـ قوله : (يقبلهما) قال الرملي أي أبو يوسف في قوله الأخير : وإذا قبلت يقضى بها على المشتري خاصة ولا يكون له أن يرجع على البائع، وبيان إمكان القضاء بها في حق الكافر أن يقضي بالملك للمدعي بسبب جديد من جهة المدعى عليه قوله : ( وكذا لو شهد رجل وامرأتان من المسلمين ويترك على دينه قال الرملي والوجه فيه أنه لو قبلت للزم القتل بشهادة رجل وامرأتين تأمل.
His statement: (Then two-thirds are for him and the remainder between them) meaning the two-thirds are for the single Muslim and the remainder for the Muslim and the Christian; it is mentioned in al-Dhakhira the phrase of al-Jami' and he did not explain its reasoning, then he mentioned another case: A Christian died leaving one thousand dirhams, and a Muslim brought witnesses from the Christians for one thousand against the deceased, and another Christian [brought] likewise; he pays the thousand left to the Muslim, and they do not share proportionally according to him, and according to Abu Yusuf they share proportionally. The difference goes back to the fact that the evidence of the Christian is accepted according to him in proving the debt against the deceased, not in proving the partnership between him and the Muslim, and according to the second opinion it is accepted in both. End. But the reason for the single Muslim's exclusive two-thirds in our case remains; and perhaps it is that the evidence requires that each of the three claimants has a third of the hundred, but the second testimony does not prove the Christian's partnership with each of the Muslims, so the third that the single Muslim was entitled to returns, and nothing returns to the other Muslim because he acknowledges that he has a right in the property to the extent of his right and that of this Christian, and he shares with him in the third he took due to his acknowledgement that he is his partner in what he has against the deceased, so he did not establish his participation by evidence. Ponder. Then I saw al-Ramli said the expression of al-Talkhis: A disbeliever died leaving one hundred, and a Muslim brought two disbelievers for one hundred, and a Muslim and a disbeliever brought likewise; then two-thirds are for the single and one-third for the two partners, the opposite of if the single were a disbeliever and the witnesses of the two partners were Muslims, because the testimony of the disbeliever is proof for the Muslim, not against him, so each Muslim is given his due first, and each disbeliever [gets] from the remainder as in debt of health and illness, and the partner divides with his partner but by claim, not by testimony. End. His statement: (he accepts them) al-Ramli said: i.e., Abu Yusuf in his last statement: when accepted, judgment is passed by it against the buyer alone, and he has no recourse against the seller, and explanation of the possibility of passing judgment by it in the case of the disbeliever is that he judges ownership to the claimant by a new cause from the defendant. His statement: (and likewise if a man and two women from the Muslims testified and he leaves him on his religion) al-Ramli said: the reasoning is that if it were accepted, it would necessitate killing by the testimony of a man and two women. Ponder.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage continues the commentary on the case from Talkhis al-Jami'. The author explains that in the case of a deceased Christian leaving 100 dirhams, with a Muslim claimant alone (with two dhimmi witnesses) and a joint claim by a Muslim and a Christian (with two dhimmi witnesses), the single Muslim gets two-thirds and the other two share the remaining third. This is based on the principle that dhimmi testimony is accepted to prove debt against a dhimmi, but not to prove partnership between a Muslim and a dhimmi. The author then contrasts this with another case: a deceased Christian leaving 1000 dirhams, a Muslim and a Christian each claim a debt. According to Abu Hanifa, the Muslim gets the whole amount and they do not share; according to Abu Yusuf, they share proportionally. The reason for the difference is that Abu Yusuf accepts the dhimmi's testimony to prove both the debt and the partnership, while Abu Hanifa only accepts it to prove the debt, not the partnership. The author then provides a detailed justification for the first case, using the idea that each claimant is entitled to a third, but the second testimony does not prove the Christian's partnership with the other Muslim. Al-Ramli then quotes a similar case from al-Talkhis, explaining that if the single claimant is a Muslim and the partners are a Muslim and a disbeliever, the Muslim gets two-thirds and the partners share one-third; but if the single is a disbeliever and the partners are Muslims, it is reversed because dhimmi testimony is proof for a Muslim but not against him. Finally, the author discusses two subsidiary rulings: first, a dhimmi's testimony accepted (according to Abu Yusuf's later view) leads to judgment only against the buyer, not against the seller; second, the testimony of a man and two women from Muslims against a dhimmi is not accepted for hadd punishments because it would lead to execution based on lesser evidence.

Key Terms
التحاصص (al-tahassus): Proportional distribution among creditors when assets are insufficient.
البينة (al-bayyinah): Evidence, consisting of testimony of witnesses.
الإقرار (al-iqrar): Acknowledgement or admission of a claim.
الشركة (al-shirkah): Partnership or joint ownership in a claim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case of a deceased dhimmi with multiple creditors, why does Abu Hanifa give the entire estate to a single Muslim claimant when both he and a dhimmi present dhimmi witnesses?
A) Because dhimmi testimony is not accepted against a Muslim, so the dhimmi cannot prove his claim against the Muslim.
B) Because dhimmi testimony can prove debt but not partnership between a Muslim and a dhimmi.
C) Because the Muslim's witnesses are considered more reliable.
D) Because the dhimmi claimant did not present witnesses.

Key Takeaway
Abu Hanifa restricts dhimmi testimony to proving debt only, not partnership with a Muslim, while Abu Yusuf accepts it for both.

Subsidiary Rulings: Acceptance of Dhimmi Testimony and Witnesses for Hadd
Subsidiary rulings on dhimmi testimony and witness requirements.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the implications of Abu Yusuf's last opinion on dhimmi testimony and identify when a man and two women's testimony is not accepted in hadd cases.

Core Idea
Abu Yusuf's later acceptance of dhimmi testimony leads to judgment against the buyer only; testimony of a man and two women is not accepted for hadd.

This lesson continues the commentary with two additional rulings. First, al-Ramli explains Abu Yusuf's final position on accepting dhimmi testimony, limiting its effect to the buyer. Second, the testimony of a man and two women from Muslims is examined when directed against a dhimmi, leading to the conclusion that it cannot establish a hadd punishment.

Guided Path
1
Understand the first subsidiary case
The case from al-Talkhis: a disbeliever dies leaving 100, a Muslim brings two disbelievers for a claim of 100, and a Muslim and a disbeliever bring likewise. The ruling is that if the single claimant is Muslim, he gets two-thirds, and the partners share one-third, but if the single is a disbeliever, it is reversed.
The key is the principle: dhimmi testimony is proof for a Muslim, not against him.
2
Apply the principle to the second case
In the case of two individual claimants (Muslim and dhimmi), each with dhimmi witnesses, the Muslim's claim is proven first, and the dhimmi's claim is only proven if there is a remainder. This is analogous to the rules of debt in health and illness.
3
Examine Abu Yusuf's last opinion
Al-Ramli says that Abu Yusuf in his last statement accepts dhimmi testimony, but judgment is passed against the buyer only, not the seller. The buyer cannot recourse against the seller.
This limits the effect of acceptance.
4
Analyze the man and two women testimony
If a man and two women from Muslims testify against a dhimmi for a hadd offense, their testimony is not accepted because it would lead to execution based on such evidence, which is insufficient for hadd.
Hadd requires stricter evidence.

Source Text

Shahadat — Testimony

8 min
Debt Priority with Dhimmi Witnesses in Mixed Claims
Ruling on debt priority with dhimmi witnesses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on debt priority when dhimmi witnesses testify for a non-Muslim claimant, and compare it with Abu Yusuf's alternative proportional distribution.

Core Idea
When dhimmi witnesses testify for a non-Muslim, the Muslim's debt is paid first — but Abu Yusuf later ruled proportionally.

In cases where a deceased non-Muslim owes debts to both a Muslim and a non-Muslim, the type of witnesses can affect distribution priority. The Minhaj presents a clear rule: if the non-Muslim's witnesses are dhimmis, the Muslim's debt is satisfied first. However, an intra-madhhab difference exists with Abu Yusuf's later proportional approach. This lesson unpacks the scenario and the underlying principles.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
A Christian dies. A Muslim and a Christian each present evidence of a debt against the deceased. Determine the status of the witnesses: are they Muslims or dhimmis?
The specific ruling depends on the witnesses' religion.
2
State the Minhaj ruling
According to al-Minhaj, if both parties' witnesses, or at least the Christian's witnesses, are dhimmis, the Muslim's debt is paid first. Any remainder goes to the Christian.
This applies even if the Muslim's witnesses are also dhimmis? The text says 'if the witnesses of both parties are dhimmis, or the witnesses of the Christian are dhimmis'.
3
Identify the intra-Hanafi difference
Abu Yusuf, in his later opinion as narrated by al-Hasan, held that the estate should be divided proportionally between the two debts.
This is recorded as his last opinion on the matter.
4
Discuss the basis of the ruling
The Minhaj's priority rule reflects a principle that dhimmi testimony may be accepted against a non-Muslim but is not given equal weight against a Muslim. Abu Yusuf's view emphasizes fairness between creditors.
The text does not provide explicit dalil; the reasoning is implicit.
5
Determine the operative position
The text presents both views without explicit tarjih. In later Hanafi practice, Abu Yusuf's proportional view is often considered after his retraction, but the Minhaj's view remains in the text.
A mufti would need to consider the context and later tarjih works.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي المنهاج للعلامة أبي حفص عمر نصراني مات فجاء مسلم ونصراني وأقام كل واحد منهما البينة أن له على الميت ديناً، فإن كان شهود الفريقين ذميين أو شهود النصراني ذميين بدئ بدين المسلم، فإن فضل شيء صرف إلى دين النصراني. وروى الحسن عن أبي يوسف أنه يجعل بينهما على قدر دينهما قيل إنه قول أبي يوسف الأخير. وإن كان شهود الفريقين مسلمين أو شهود الذمي
And in al-Minhaj by the scholar Abu Hafs Umar: A Christian died, and a Muslim and a Christian came, each presenting evidence that he has a debt against the deceased. If the witnesses of both parties are dhimmis, or the witnesses of the Christian are dhimmis, the Muslim's debt is started with; if anything remains, it is given to the Christian's debt. And al-Hasan narrated from Abu Yusuf that he apportions between them according to the amount of their debts; it is said that this is Abu Yusuf's last opinion. And if the witnesses of both parties are Muslims, or the witnesses of the dhimmi...
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage, from al-Minhaj by Abu Hafs Umar, presents a further case within the chapter on testimony. When a Christian dies and a Muslim and a Christian each claim a debt, the prioritization of the Muslim's claim depends on the nature of the witnesses. If the witnesses are dhimmis (both parties' or at least the Christian's), the Muslim's debt is paid first; any remainder goes to the Christian. This is consistent with the principle that dhimmi testimony is valid for a Muslim but not against him. However, Abu Yusuf reportedly held that they should share proportionally, and this is considered his last opinion. The passage is cut off, but it likely discussed the scenario where witnesses are Muslims or the dhimmi's witnesses are Muslims, where different rules might apply. The underlying rule is that testimony favorable to a Muslim is more readily accepted.

Key Terms
المنهاج (al-Minhaj): A later Hanafi fiqh work by Abu Hafs Umar al-Nasafi or al-Turkumani.
بدئ بدين المسلم (budi'a bi-dayn al-Muslim): The Muslim's debt is given priority in payment.
القدر (al-qadr): Proportion; distribution according to the amount of debt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Minhaj, if a Christian dies and both a Muslim and a Christian claim debts, and the Christian's witnesses are dhimmis, how is the estate distributed?
A) The Muslim and Christian share equally.
B) The Christian's debt is paid first, then the Muslim's.
C) The Muslim's debt is paid first; any remainder goes to the Christian.
D) The estate is divided proportionally based on debt amounts.

Key Takeaway
When dhimmi witnesses testify for a non-Muslim claimant, the Muslim's debt is prioritized, but Abu Yusuf's proportional view is an important intra-madhhab alternative.

Shahadat — Testimony
intermediate
10 min
Testimony of Non-Muslims in Debt Claims Against a Deceased Christian
Explains rulings on testimony of non-Muslims in debt claims.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on precedence between a Muslim and a Christian claimant when both produce non-Muslim witnesses, and identify the final position of Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
Non-Muslim testimony can benefit a Muslim claimant but never harm him, so the Muslim is paid first.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq explains a nuanced area of Hanafi fiqh: how to adjudicate debt claims against a deceased Christian when both a Muslim and a Christian present evidence. The core principle is that the testimony of a non-Muslim is accepted only when it supports a Muslim's claim or concerns another non-Muslim. We will trace the various scenarios and the intra-Hanafi disagreement between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf on how to prioritize claimants.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and parties
Determine who is the deceased (Christian), the claimants, and their witnesses. Key terms: munfarid (isolated claimant of one religion) and sharikayn (partners, one Muslim one Christian).
2
Classify the witnesses
Are the witnesses Muslims or dhimmis? This determines the application of the rule: testimony of a disbeliever is proof for a Muslim, not against him.
3
Apply the precedence rule
If the Muslim claimant's witnesses are dhimmis or Muslims, his claim is satisfied first. If both claimants have Muslim witnesses, they are equal (initial view) or the Muslim still gets precedence (final view of Abu Yusuf).
Muhammad al-Shaybani reports that Abu Yusuf's final opinion gives precedence to the Muslim.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute
Abu Yusuf initially held that both claimants are equal regardless of witnesses, but retracted. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is the Muslim's precedence.
This is an example of intra-madhhab ikhtilaf resolved by later authority (al-Ramli).
5
Consider the mathematical distribution
When there are multiple claimants and mixed witnesses, calculate shares: the isolated Muslim gets two-thirds, the partners (Muslim and Christian) share one-third. This reverses if the isolated claimant is a Christian and the partners' witnesses are Muslim.
The author explains the logic using an example of a 100 dirham debt.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

باطلة لأن . أنه مرتد ولا شهادة لأهل الذمة على المرتد ا هـ . وفي المحيط : تقبل شهادة الكافر على زعمهم وفي من المسلم المنفرد بالثلثين في مسألتنا ولعله هو أن البينة تقتضي أن لكل من الثلاثة المدعين ثلث المائة لكن الشهادة الثانية لا تثبت مشاركة النصراني لكل من المسلمين فيعود الثلث الذي كان يستحقه للمسلم المنفرد، وإنما لا يعود منه للمسلم الآخر شيء لأنه مقر بأن له حقاً في المال بقدر حقه ولهذا النصراني ويقاسمه في الثلث الذي أخذه لإقراره بأنه شريكه فيما له على الميت فلم تكن يرجع مشاركته له بالبينة تأمل. ثم رأيت الرملي قال عبارة التلخيص : كافر مات عن مائة فأقام مسلم كافرين بمائة وأقام مسلم وكافر كذلك فثلثاها للمنفرد والثلث للشريكين عكس ما لو كان المنفرد كافراً وشهودا الشريكين مسلمان لأن شهادة الكافر حجة للمسلم لا عليه فضرب كل مسلم فيها بقدر حقه أولاً وكل كافر في الباقي كما في دين الصحة والمرض And in al-Khulasa: If the assignor was a Christian, then one-third is for him and the remainder for the two [others], and the Muslim is given precedence. Similarly, if the witnesses of the two partners were Muslims and their witnesses were two Christians or two Muslims, they are equal. [Case:] A Christian died leaving two sons, one of whom became Muslim; then a Muslim produced two Christian witnesses after his death, and his estate was divided for a debt owed to him: it is taken from the share of the non-Muslim due to lack of proof against him, like his own acknowledgment. And if the Muslim produced two dhimmis and a dhimmi like them [claimed], the Muslim is given precedence; from Abu Yusuf: they are equal. Muhammad said: that is his last opinion. Based on this, if he was alive and they claimed a specific item in his hand, and from him [narrated] that it is for the Muslim, and distinguished by its connection to the place. End. And in al-Majma': If a dhimmi bought a house from a Muslim, then a dhimmi or a Muslim claimed it with testimony of two dhimmis, he accepts them against [the buyer's] right but rejects them [against the seller]. End. From Expressions of Disbelief: Two Christians testified against a Christian that he had become Muslim while he denied: their testimony is not permissible. Likewise, if a man and two women from the Muslims testified, he is left on his religion; all people of disbelief are equal in this. And if two Christians testified that a Christian woman had become Muslim, it is permissible, and she is compelled to Islam but not killed. This is all the statement of Abu Hanifa. End. al-Muhit al-Burhani: If a man and two women from the Muslims testified to the Islam of a Christian while he denied, he is compelled to Islam and [their testimony] is not accepted. And if two men of his own religion testified while he denied, their testimony is invalid because he is an apostate, and there is no testimony of ahl al-dhimma against an apostate. End. And in al-Muhit: The testimony of a disbeliever is accepted according to their claim, and [regarding] the Muslim who is alone with two-thirds in our issue, perhaps it is that the evidence requires that each of the three claimants has one-third of the hundred, but the second testimony does not prove the Christian's partnership with each of the Muslims, so the one-third that was due to the isolated Muslim returns, and nothing from it returns to the other Muslim because he acknowledged that he has a right in the property equivalent to his right and that of this Christian, and he shares with him in the one-third he took due to his acknowledgment that he is his partner in what he [the deceased] owed him, so his partnering him by evidence did not revert. Ponder. Then I saw al-Ramli said: The wording of al-Talkhis: A disbeliever died leaving one hundred [dirhams]; a Muslim came with two disbelievers claiming one hundred, and a Muslim and a disbeliever came similarly; then two-thirds are for the isolated one and one-third for the two partners, opposite to the case where the isolated one was a disbeliever and the witnesses of the two partners were Muslim, because the testimony of a disbeliever is a proof for the Muslim, not against him. So each Muslim is given his share first, and each disbeliever [takes] from the remainder, like in debt of health and illness. And the partner shares with his partner, but by a claim [zaghm] not by testimony. End. His statement: (He accepts them) al-Ramli said: i.e., Abu Yusuf in his last opinion: When it is accepted, judgment is given by it against the buyer specifically, and he cannot recourse against the seller. And the explanation of the possibility of judging by it against the disbeliever is that he judges the ownership to the claimant through a new cause from the defendant's side. His statement: (Likewise, if a man and two women from the Muslims testified and he is left on his religion) al-Ramli said: The reasoning is that if it were accepted, it would necessitate killing by the testimony of a man and two women, reflect. And in al-Minhaj of al-Allamah Abu Hafs Umar: A Christian died; a Muslim and a Christian came, and each one produced evidence that he has a debt against the deceased. If the witnesses of both parties were dhimmis, or the witnesses of the Christian were dhimmis, the Muslim's debt is given precedence; if anything remains, it is given to the Christian's debt. And al-Hasan narrated from Abu Yusuf that he makes it between them according to their debts; it is said that this is Abu Yusuf's last opinion. And if the witnesses of both parties were Muslims, or the witnesses of the dhimmi...
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of rulings from multiple sources on the testimony of non-Muslims (dhimmis and Christians) in debt claims following the death of a Christian debtor. The author begins with a case from al-Khulasa: when a Muslim and a Christian both claim a debt from a deceased Christian, and the Christian's witnesses are also Christians, the Muslim is given precedence (i.e., his claim is satisfied first). If both claimants have Muslim witnesses, they are equal. In a scenario where a Christian dies leaving two sons (one Muslim) and a Muslim brings two Christian witnesses to prove a debt, the debt is taken from the non-Muslim son's share because there is no proof against him otherwise. This mirrors an acknowledgment. When a Muslim brings two dhimmis and the opponent is also a dhimmi, the Muslim prevails. Abu Yusuf initially held equality, but his final view (according to Muhammad) is precedence for the Muslim. There is also a specialized case from al-Majma' about a dhimmi buying a house from a Muslim and a subsequent claim; the testimony of two dhimmis is accepted against the buyer but not the seller. The text then discusses testimony regarding conversion to Islam: if two Christians testify that a fellow Christian has embraced Islam but he denies, their testimony is rejected. However, if the same is testified by a Muslim man and two women, the alleged convert is left on his original religion (not compelled to Islam, according to Abu Hanifa). If two Christians testify that a Christian woman became Muslim, it is accepted; she is compelled to Islam but not executed. The reasoning is that apostasy (riddah) is not proven by dhimmi testimony, but conversion to Islam for a woman does not carry the death penalty. A later passage from al-Muhit al-Burhani clarifies that testimony of Muslim witnesses against a denying Christian compels him to Islam but is not accepted (i.e., the testimony itself is not admitted; rather, the judge compels based on other grounds), while testimony of coreligionists is invalid because it would prove apostasy. The commentary then centers on a mathematical distribution when a disbeliever dies with a 100 debt and various witnesses. Al-Ramli's explanation from al-Talkhis is cited: when the isolated claimant is Muslim and the partners (Muslim and Christian) have mixed witnesses, the Muslim isolated gets two-thirds, the partners share one-third. This reverses if the isolated is Christian and partners' witnesses are Muslim, because Christian testimony helps Muslims, not harms them. The author then explains the logic: each Muslim first receives his due proportion, then the Christian takes from the remainder. The final part of the passage from al-Minhaj reiterates the principle: in a dispute between a Muslim and a Christian over a debt from a deceased, if witnesses are dhimmis, the Muslim is paid first. If both parties have Muslim witnesses, or the Muslim's witnesses are Muslims and the Christian's are dhimmis, they are equal (in some narrations) or the Muslim is given precedence (in Abu Yusuf's last opinion). This passage is a detailed demonstration of Hanafi jurisprudence on the acceptance of non-Muslim testimony only when it does not harm a Muslim's right.

Key Terms
المنفرد (al-munfarid): The isolated claimant who is the only one of his religion (Muslim or Christian) in a group of claimants.
الشريكين (al-sharīkayn): The two claimants who are partners in a claim, one Muslim and one Christian.
ذمي (dhimmi): A non-Muslim citizen of the Islamic state, usually Christian or Jew, who is protected under Islamic law.
شهادة (shahādah): Testimony given in court, subject to conditions of integrity and number of witnesses.
بينة (bayyinah): Evidence, usually two witnesses, that proves a claim in court.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case of a deceased Christian with two claimants (one Muslim, one Christian), if the Muslim brings two Christian witnesses and the Christian brings two Muslims, what is the distribution according to the final view of Abu Yusuf?
A) The Muslim gets nothing, the Christian gets everything.
B) The Muslim gets two-thirds, the Christian gets one-third.
C) They split equally.
D) The Muslim gets the entire amount.

Key Takeaway
The core principle: non-Muslim testimony is proof for Muslims, not against them; hence a Muslim claimant is always given precedence over a non-Muslim claimant in debt claims against a deceased non-Muslim.

Shahadat — Testimony
intermediate
